Over the last several years, [Ottawa]{.underline} has [failed to
articulate **any meaningful strategy** in response to major global
events]{.underline}, while at the same time [jeopardizing]{.underline}
some of Canada's most important [diplomatic relations]{.underline}. This
should set alarm bells off in Washington. If Canadian and U.S. foreign
policy remain this misaligned, the consequences for America could be
serious.

Consider, for instance, how Canada and the U.S. have approached India
and Israel. Both New Delhi and Jerusalem are critical pillars of the
global democratic architecture. Both face relentless threats from
nonstate actors and revisionist regimes. Both are central to the future
of democratic cooperation.

India is a key partner in pushing back against China's Indo-Pacific
dominance, while Israel is the only liberal democracy in the Middle
East.

The U.S. has, for the most part, treated both Israel and India with
consistency and clarity. After the Oct. 7 Hamas attacks, America
affirmed Israel's right to self-defense while engaging actively on
humanitarian concerns. Likewise, when hitmen allegedly linked to the
Indian government assassinated in British Columbia Hardeep Singh Nijjar,
a leader in the Sikh separatist movement, Washington remained cautious
and disciplined, allowing its intelligence agencies to manage the matter
discreetly while protecting its strategic partnership with India.

[Canada]{.underline}, by contrast, [flinched under domestic
pressure]{.underline} in both cases, [allowing diaspora politics and
electoral calculations to distort its diplomatic posture, souring
relations with]{.underline} two of its most [important
allies]{.underline}. Prime Minister Justin Trudeau's public accusation
of Indian state involvement in Nijjar's killing derailed already fragile
ties, [leading]{.underline} Indian Prime Minister Narendra [Modi to
harshly criticize Canada and shun Trudeau]{.underline} at the 2023 G20
summit.

A similar drift is evident in Canada's response to Israel's war with
Hamas. Ottawa initially condemned the terrorist attacks. But in the face
of rising domestic protest and activist pressure, the government's
stance became incoherent --- a muddled blend of contradictory U.N.
votes, hedged statements and an inability to affirm basic principles
such as Israel's right to self-defense. Canada's new prime minister,
Mark Carney, has even echoed the charge that Israel is committing
genocide in Gaza.

Beyond weakening key strategic alliances, [Canada has **not communicated
a clear vision** about the world's most pressing conflict
zones]{.underline}. For instance, [Ottawa]{.underline} has [repeatedly
capitulated to]{.underline} demands from [the Chinese government, while
remaining]{.underline} shockingly [noncommittal when it comes
to]{.underline} defending [Taiwan]{.underline} from a possible Chinese
invasion. [**Waffling like a feather in the wind**, Canada
was]{.underline} also [the last member of]{.underline} the [Five
Eyes]{.underline} intelligence alliance [to ban Chinese
government-connected companies]{.underline} Huawei and ZTE [from its 5G
networks]{.underline}. And while Beijing continues to carry out
everything from widespread foreign interference operations to espionage
to executing Canadian citizens on Chinese soil, Canadian officials seem
fine focusing on "positive developments" between the two countries.

[On Ukraine]{.underline}, statements of support are clear, but
**[material and financial assistance is lacking]{.underline}**, and
[Canada has]{.underline}, once again, [**avoided any clear long-term
strategy** for the region]{.underline}. And despite a storied history of
peacekeeping, demilitarizing war zones and brokering peace agreements,
[Ottawa's approach to Israel's]{.underline} multifront [war]{.underline}
in the Middle East [is **virtually nonexistent**]{.underline}.

Going forward, Canadian leaders must rediscover that [trust and
reliability mean treating allies and issues not as political footballs,
but as long-term partners and problems in a dangerous multipolar
world]{.underline}. If they do not, America might have to make some
tough decisions.

For instance, if Canada cannot be trusted to fully back Israel in its
greatest time of need, can America count on Canada as a credible
security partner? Further, if Canada fails to muster up the courage to
more forcefully confront China, Washington will have no choice but to
reevaluate everything from economic ties to defense cooperation with
Ottawa. [Canada]{.underline} also [continues to **spend a pittance not
just on its own military, but on support for key allies**]{.underline}
such as Ukraine and alliances like NATO. In time, this could increase
pressure for the U.S. to make up for Canada's shortcomings --- something
it should not be expected to do.

All told, while the U.S. under President [Trump]{.underline} has made a
number of shocking [blunders]{.underline} when it comes to allyship and
global engagement, that **[does not excuse the strategic dysfunction of
countries like Canada]{.underline}**. In fact, it probably requires
allies such as Canada to step up and play an outsized role to preserve
the order we've long taken for granted under U.S. leadership.

At a time when the U.S. faces serious global challenges and the stakes
are this high, **[a confused and reactive Canada is one liability
America cannot afford]{.underline}**.

### Canada Fails\-\--1AR

#### They're [indifferent]{.underline} towards leadership.

Kim Richard **Nossal 24**, Professor Emeritus of Political Studies at
Queen's University, Ph.D. in Political Science from the University of
Toronto, "Realism, Romanticism, or Indifference? Canada's Geostrategic
Choices in an Era of Great-Power Rivalry," in *Canada and Competing
Indo-Pacific Visions of China and the US: Caught in a Foreign Policy
Dilemma*, pp. 103-121, Palgrave Macmillan, 2024, accessed 07-25-2025,
https://doi.org/10.1007/978-3-031-71785-7, DG

But there is a third pole that we have seen emerge at different times in
Canadian history---what the international relations scholar Arnold
Wolfers theorized as the "pole of indifference." Wolfers argued that
while some states might evince "a frantic concern for the enhancement of
power," it was possible, at least theoretically, that [some
states]{.underline} could [demonstrate a]{.underline} "**[complete
indifference]{.underline}**" **[to power]{.underline}**. Indeed, he
cited the Canadian-American relationship as an example of such
indifference at work (Wolfers 1951, pp. 56 and 57). But it can be argued
that [Canadian indifference to power]{.underline} often [goes well
beyond North America]{.underline}. In this view, [Canadians
are]{.underline} essentially **[unconcerned about global
politics]{.underline}** more broadly. [This can]{.underline} perhaps
best [be seen in electoral outcomes. Canadians]{.underline} tend to
[elect]{.underline} Members of Parliament ([MPs]{.underline}) who are
[committed to an]{.underline} "**[a-strategic]{.underline}**"
**[approach]{.underline}** to global politics (Nossal 2016)---in other
words, [they have little inclination to grapple with]{.underline} the
[hard geostrategic choices]{.underline} about how best to respond to
great-power politics; instead, [they]{.underline} prefer to [concentrate
their attention on **highly local issues** like reducing auto theft, or
solving housing shortages]{.underline}, or "growing the middle class."

In this view, [indifference impels Canadians to **avoid spending their
wealth on global politics**. They]{.underline} certainly [do not want to
spend on]{.underline} the [hard power resources]{.underline} that are
[required]{.underline} by the needs of Realpolitik. Instead, for much of
their history Canadians have adhered to the geostrategic wisdom of David
Mills, a Liberal MP from Ontario, who in 1875 outlined his particular
"security imaginary" (Nossal 2010) to the House of Commons:

> In a country situated as we are, not likely to be involved in war, and
> having a large demand upon our resources for public improvements, it
> \[is\] highly desirable to have our military affairs conducted as
> cheaply as possible. (quoted in Morton 1987, p. 643)

In just thirty-nine words, Mills captured the core reasons why Canadians
have the attitudes they do toward defense. [Canada's geostrategic
location]{.underline}---sep[arated from the rest of the
wor]{.underline}ld by three oceans, [with a harshly inhospitable polar
region]{.underline} on its northern approaches [and the United
States]{.underline}, a dominant and now hegemonic power, [as its
southern neighbor]{.underline}---[means]{.underline} that [Canadians
have the **extraordinary luxury** of being able to devote their wealth
to things other than defense]{.underline}, so that whatever defense
needs to be done can---and should---be done on the cheap. It is clear
that successive generations of Canadians have agreed. But [if Canadians
are complacent about national security, neglectful of the armed forces,
indifferent to great-power politics, and]{.underline}, above all,
[cheap]{.underline} and unwilling to spend [on international affairs, it
is because **history has shown them that**]{.underline}, given Canada's
geostrategic location in North America, **[they do not need to take
defense seriously]{.underline}**. As the historian Desmond Morton has
pointed out, Canadians have never suffered from the indifference that
they have persistently exhibited to global politics (Morton 1987, p.
628).

**[Nor do Canadians want to spend the money]{.underline}** that a
romantic "do-good" **[foreign policy requires]{.underline}**. As a
result, Canadians who are indifferent to power politics also tend to
favor a romantic version of foreign policy that costs as little as
possible. As Gotlieb put it, we have seen the move from "a do-good to a
feel-good foreign policy" (Gotlieb 2005, p. 18), [with Canadian voters
rewarding politicians who spend little on]{.underline} foreign and
[defense]{.underline} policy, [but instead treat international policy as
**performative politics**, feeding back to their constituents a romantic
foreign policy]{.underline}---**[fanciful tales of Canada's role in the
world]{.underline}** that are [pleasing to Canadian ears and make
Canadians feel good about themselves]{.underline} (Stairs 2003a, 2003b;
Nossal 2005).

#### They lack diplomatic firepower, and other countries don't like them. 

Jeffrey B. **Kucharski 24**, Senior Fellow at the Macdonald-Laurier
Institute, Ph.D. from Kyoto University, "Smart Power and Canada's
Effectiveness in the Indo-Pacific," in *Canada and Competing
Indo-Pacific Visions of China and the US: Caught in a Foreign Policy
Dilemma*, pp. 145-181, Palgrave Macmillan, 2024, accessed 07-25-2025,
https://doi.org/10.1007/978-3-031-71785-7, DG

[Canada possesses significant diplomatic resources]{.underline} around
the world, with an international presence in about 180 countries in
embassies, consulates, or commissions. Canada's trade commissioners and
diplomats interact with business and political contacts at multiple
levels in seeking to promote trade and investment and advance Canada's
broader interests. Global Affairs Canada also employs public diplomacy
efforts to enhance Canada's image abroad and promote Canada and the
"Canada brand."

[Consistency and stability **have not been a hallmark** of leadership at
Canada's foreign ministry]{.underline}. Canada's current foreign
minister is the fifth in six years. In December, [Canada's
Senate]{.underline} published a report asking whether Canada's foreign
service was "fit for purpose." The report [noted]{.underline} that [the
**lack of investment and recruitment** in Canada's diplomatic capacities
and capabilities]{.underline} has **[undermined Canada's ability to
influence and shape global issues and effect change]{.underline}**
(Senate of Canada 2023). In this context, Global Affairs Canada recently
announced a three-year transformation plan to help ensure it is properly
equipped to meet the global challenges Canada faces now and into the
future (Global Affairs Canada 2023a). Through the IPS, capacity is also
being established abroad and in Canada to support expanded regional and
bilateral engagement, including new foreign service positions created
and staffed in various Indo-Pacific countries and additional
China-related capacity (Global Affairs Canada 2024).

[Despite being the country that literally invented]{.underline} the
modern concept of [peacekeeping, Canada]{.underline} currently [ranks
70th on the UN list of peacekeeping personnel, contributing fewer than
60]{.underline} uniformed personnel to UN peacekeeping missions (United
Nations 2023). Canada recently declined a request from the United States
to send a peacekeeping force to Haiti which is wracked by gang violence,
noting that it lacks the capacity to do so.

[Canada's track record on agenda-setting has been a **mix of successes
and failures**.]{.underline} Canada in recent years has developed a
reputation for global leadership in promoting universal human rights, an
open international trading system and strengthening the rules-based
international order. In response to the "two-Michael's" affair, Canada
exerted effective leadership in marshaling international support for the
Declaration on Arbitrary Detentions and demonstrated leadership in
finalizing a global biodiversity agreement in Montreal in 2022. On the
other hand, [Canada failed to secure a UN Security Council seat on two
consecutive bids]{.underline}, under both the current and previous
governments. 11 [Canada]{.underline} also [**suffers from poor
diplomatic relations** with]{.underline} two of the world's largest
countries, [China and India, both of whom are critical to Canada's
future]{.underline} in the Indo-Pacific.

#### Large powers [don't listen]{.underline}. 

Andrew **Latham 24**, Professor of International Relations at Macalester
College, Senior Fellow at the Institute for Peace and Diplomacy, Ph.D.
in Political Science from York University, "Canada's 'middle power'
diplomacy no longer works in China," The Hill, 08-06-2024, accessed
07-25-2025,
https://thehill.com/opinion/international/4811666-canadas-middle-power-diplomacy-no-longer-works-in-china,
DG

In an increasingly multipolar world, [the siren song of Canada's middle
power diplomacy has]{.underline} effectively [been **drowned out by**
the noise of **great power competition**]{.underline}. The rise of
[China]{.underline}, the resurgence of [Russia and]{.underline} the
ongoing [assertiveness of other regional powers]{.underline} have
[created a complex and challenging international
environment]{.underline} in which [Canada's traditional approach is
**ill-suited** to advance its interests]{.underline}.

Moreover, [Canada's middle power status has been]{.underline} further
[eroded by]{.underline} the [declining]{.underline} influence of
[**multilat**eral institutions]{.underline}, a key arena within which
Canada once pursued its middle power diplomacy. The rise of [nationalism
and protectionism, coupled with the]{.underline} increasing
[assertiveness of non-Western powers, has undermined the efficacy of
traditional multilateral forums]{.underline}. As a result, **[Canada's
ability to shape global outcomes]{.underline}** through multilateral
cooperation **[has been largely diminished]{.underline}**.

Similarly, [with respect to regional security]{.underline} issues,
[Canada]{.underline} simply [lacks the **standing, leverage and moral
authority** to engage]{.underline} China [in a serious
negotiation]{.underline} around Indo-Pacific security. [Canada is **not
a consequential**]{.underline} regional security
**[player]{.underline}** in the Indo-Pacific. Or, put slightly
differently, Canada is not now and likely never will be an Indo-Pacific
"middle power" in the current sense of that term --- that is, a power
defined by its rootedness in the Indo-Pacific region, by its
considerable economic and military capacity relative to other
Indo-Pacific states or by its historical and cultural pedigree as a
member of the Indo-Pacific civilization.

This being the case, nothing Foreign Minister Joly said to Foreign
Minister Wang on the topic of global or regional security is likely to
have had the slightest effect on China's grand strategy, regional policy
or global military posture. If she did raise these issues, it would have
been the diplomatic equivalent of, in the words of one Chinese proverb,
"playing a harp to a cow."

So, [unlike during the]{.underline} long-past [heyday of Canadian middle
power diplomacy, in today's world, **no one**]{.underline} --- and
especially not Beijing --- **[pays any attention whatsoever to what
Ottawa has to say about global and regional security
issues]{.underline}**.

To be sure, this is painful for Canadians to accept. But it is the hard,
cold reality that they must acknowledge and embrace if they actually
want to advance and defend their national interests in the real world.
[To cling to nostalgic notions that Canada is an indispensable middle
power that punches above its weight on the international stage is
**hallucinatory**]{.underline}.

I hope Joly devoted all of her time in Beijing to addressing key
bilateral issues related to Canada's narrow national interests. And I
hope she did so in a way that did not make her sound like the foreign
minister of Yelang.

#### Lack of defense spending undermines their credibility. 

John **Ibbitson 25**, Award-Winning Political Writer at The Globe and
Mail, "Declining stature of foreign affairs minister underscores
Canada's waning influence on world stage," Fraser Institute, 06-02-2025,
accessed 07-25-2025,
https://www.fraserinstitute.org/commentary/declining-stature-foreign-affairs-minister-underscores-canadas-waning-influence-world,
DG

In the cabinet he unveiled in May, Prime Minister Mark Carney appointed
Anita Anand to serve as this year's foreign affairs minister.

Yes, that's tongue in cheek. Anand may well serve longer than one year
in that role. Her predecessor, Mélanie Joly, served three-and-a-half
years, almost as long as John Baird who served from May 2011 to February
2015. But those two were exceptions to what has become a revolving door
ministry. [In this century, 15 people have served as minister of foreign
affairs]{.underline}.

Some of this has to do with the six minority governments we've had in
Ottawa over that time. But the bigger reason is that **[Global Affairs
Canada]{.underline}**, as it is currently known, **[no longer has the
stature it once enjoyed]{.underline}**, in part because foreign policy
is directed more and more from the Prime Minister's Office, and
[because]{.underline} "**[Canada\'s standing in the world has
slipped]{.underline}**," as Marc Garneau, who was foreign minister for
nine months in 2021, wrote in his memoir. \"We are losing credibility.\"

The minister of foreign affairs was once seen almost as a Canadian
equivalent of an American vice-president. Louis St. Laurent, Lester
Pearson and Jean Chretien all served at foreign affairs before later
becoming prime minister. Joe Clark served as foreign affairs minister
after having been prime minister.

And Canadian foreign policy once mattered in world affairs. Pearson
played a key role in the negotiations that led to the creation of the
North Atlantic Treaty Organization (NATO) in 1949. He was the only
Canadian to win the Nobel Prize for Peace, after he helped broker a
ceasefire during the 1956 Suez Crisis that led to the first peacekeeping
mission.

During her brief tenure as foreign affairs minister in 1979, Flora
MacDonald promoted the idea of bringing Vietnamese refugees to Canada
through private sponsorships by citizens and groups. The United Nations
awarded Canada the Nansen Refugee Award for accepting more than 60,000
boat people, as they were known, from Indochina.

Joe Clark, as External Affairs minister in the 1980s, put Canada in the
forefront of opposition to the apartheid regime in South Africa.

At the turn of this century, foreign minister Lloyd Axworthy led the
movement that brought about the United Nations doctrine of
Responsibility to Protect---a global commitment to prevent genocide and
other atrocities.

And following the attacks on 9/11, Foreign affairs minister John Manley
worked closely with Homeland Security Director Tom Ridge to secure the
Canada-U.S. border, while continuing to promote free trade across it.

Since then, the stature of foreign affairs ministers has declined. Bill
Graham was deeply disappointed when Paul Martin replaced him as minister
with Pierre Pettigrew, whom Martin kept on a very short leash.

Stephen [Harper cycled through a plethora of foreign affairs
ministers]{.underline}---Peter MacKay, Maxime Bernier, David Emerson,
Lawrence Cannon, Baird and, in the final months, Rob Nicholson.

Justin [Trudeau did the same]{.underline}, replacing Stéphane Dion with
Chrystia Freeland, then François-Philippe Champagne, then Garneau and
finally Joly.

Part of this is the way of the world. In foreign affairs ministries
everywhere, from Foggy Bottom to Whitehall to the Quai d'Orsay,
diplomats lament that the office of the head of government effectively
runs foreign affairs, shunting them aside.

But [the declining stature of the Canada's minister of foreign affairs
coincides with the **declining stature of Canada within the Western
alliance and beyond**, which itself is the result of the **decline in
Canda's defence capability**]{.underline}.

In the 1950s, during the Korean War, Canada's defence spending reached
7.4 per cent of GDP. On John Diefenbaker's watch it sat above 4 per
cent; on Pearson's, around 3 per cent.

Pierre Trudeau reduced the size and role of Canada's military, while
seeking to make Canada less closely aligned with the United States. He
took defence spending down to 2 per cent of GDP, where Brian Mulroney
kept it.

[With the end of the Cold War, and]{.underline} with [the need to
balance the federal budget, defence spending]{.underline} under Jean
Chretien and Paul Martin [fell]{.underline} to about 1 per cent of GDP,
where it largely remained under Stephen Harper and Justin Trudeau.

[Other members of NATO are investing heavily]{.underline} in their
military in the wake of Russia's invasion of Ukraine. But **[Canada
continues to lag behind]{.underline}**.

Prime Minister Carney promises to increase Canada's defence commitments
while pursuing a more robust foreign and trade policy. We shall see. In
any case, Anand should not get too comfortable in her office at the
Pearson building. If past is precedent, she may not be there long.

#### Lack of influence and inability to coerce other countries. 

**Dang et al. 25**, Matthew Dang, M.A. candidate in Global Governance at
the University of Waterloo; Davin D'Mello, Master of International
Public Policy candidate at Wilfrid Laurier University; Lea Dörflinger,
M.A. candidate in International Studies at the University of Konstanz,
"Soft Power, Sport and Diplomacy Strategies: Potential and Opportunities
for Canada's Foreign Policy," in *The Illiberal Order: 2025 Global
Trends Report*, pp. 9-16, Balsillie School of International Affairs,
2025, accessed 07-25-2025,
https://balsillieschool.ca/wp-content/uploads/2025/01/BSIA-2025-Anthology_Illiberal-Order.pdf,
DG

However, [Canada]{.underline} has [**slipped as a middle power**. The
nation has faced a **continuous loss of influence**]{.underline} as
[shown in its failure to earn a]{.underline} United Nations [Security
Council seat]{.underline} (ibid., 3; Kemp 2023, 2). [Canada
has]{.underline} also [taken a **massive step back** in]{.underline} its
approach to [foreign policy]{.underline} as [the nation **lacks a clear
sense of direction**]{.underline} on foreign policy [and is]{.underline}
instead [producing reactive policy with **ad-hoc
approaches**]{.underline} (Kemp 2023, 4). [This]{.underline} slip in
international status [puts an even greater hinderance on the nation's
ability to use its hard power resources]{.underline}. For example,
Canada's [hard sanctioning capacities are **completely ineffective** due
to]{.underline} compounding factors of [Canada's step back as a middle
power and]{.underline} Canada's **[lack of economic or military
resources to coerce countries]{.underline}** into changing their
behaviour (Nault 2017, 11). Moreover, as a nation that still pretending
to operate as a middle power, [Canada struggles with the moral
implications of]{.underline} employing "blunt" [sanctions]{.underline}
that can lead to human rights violations in the targeted country
(Elliott 2016, 177-78; Biersteker, Tourinho and Eckert 2016, 272).
Violating human rights and negatively impacting other nations
contradicts the middle power position of upholding peace and security,
which has historically shaped the Canadian political identity (Hynek
2004, 36). Lastly, hard sanctions can disrupt Canada's social network
and create friction between Canada and its biggest allies (Nault 2017,
9, 28). [This creates extreme volatility since the world has continued
to shift toward a greater multilateral approach, creating greater
connections]{.underline} between nations [and putting Canada in a tricky
situation when attempting to take the lead on multilateral initiatives
such as hard sanctioning]{.underline} (Kemp 2023, 1).

### Canada Fails\-\--Military

#### They don't spend enough on their military.

Jeremy **Paltiel 24**, Professor of Political Science at Carleton
University, Ph.D. from the University of California, Berkeley, "Unable
to Resist: Unwilling to Take Risks---Canada in the Vice of Sino-American
Rivalry," in *Canada and Competing Indo-Pacific Visions of China and the
US: Caught in a Foreign Policy Dilemma*, pp. 183-214, Palgrave
Macmillan, 2024, accessed 07-25-2025,
https://doi.org/10.1007/978-3-031-71785-7, DG

[Making an impact and contribution to the balance of power]{.underline}
in the Indo-Pacific [is an acute question given Canada's **laggardly
commitment to NATO** and]{.underline} generally **[threadbare defense
policy]{.underline}**. Two problems challenge Canada's contributions in
the Indo-Pacific: the first is a lowball commitment to defense spending
and readiness; the second is our traditional focus on the North
Atlantic. Canada's colonial history with Great Britain and France and
commitment to the British Empire continuing right up to the Second World
War largely determined the North Atlantic anchor of our foreign and
defense policies (Nossal 2018). As far back as the Second World War,
Prime Minister Mackenzie King made the decision to concentrate our
efforts on Europe and the North Atlantic a decision which continued a
long legacy into the NATO era. In an extended period of constrained
defense spending and commitment, [NATO interoperability enmeshed and
focused Canada's military efforts along with]{.underline} a [continuing
commitment to]{.underline} continental defense through the North
American Aerospace Defense Command ([NORAD]{.underline}). **[The Pacific
was always an add-on]{.underline}**. In addition, [the vastness of the
Pacific and the distinct]{.underline} configuration of [our Pacific
coastline]{.underline} (a comparatively short straight-line distance
[squeezed between the lower 48 states]{.underline} of the United States
[and the Alaskan panhandle]{.underline}) [means]{.underline} that [there
can be no easy equivalence between the North Atlantic and the North
Pacific i]{.underline}n terms of [responsibility and commitment to
trans-oceanic sea-lanes. While Canada]{.underline} has [long claimed a
role as a Pacific nation and has recognized the growing importance of
the Asia-Pacific]{.underline} in world trade and investment, [this
was]{.underline} largely [spoken of in terms of]{.underline}
"**[opportunity]{.underline}**" **[rather than as a commitment and
responsibility]{.underline}** (Paltiel 2017). Canada's commitment to
NATO is strong, and in some sense existential, as a core part of
Canadian identity through Pearsonian internationalism. The problem is
that **[it does not feel a sufficient threat to do more than the
necessary to spend on defense]{.underline}**.

#### Its military is pathetic.

Stephen R. **Nagy 24**, Professor of Politics and International Studies
at International Christian University, Senior Fellow at the MacDonald
Laurier Institute, Ph.D. in International Relations from Waseda
University, "Reinventing Canada's Middle Power Identity: Practicing
Pragmatic Realism in an Era of US-China Strategic Competition in the
Indo-Pacific," in *Canada and Competing Indo-Pacific Visions of China
and the US: Caught in a Foreign Policy Dilemma*, pp. 85-101, Palgrave
Macmillan, 2024, accessed 07-25-2025,
https://doi.org/10.1007/978-3-031-71785-7, DG

[**Low military capability** is perhaps Canada's weakest point in its
middle power]{.underline} diplomatic [engagement]{.underline} within the
Indo-Pacific. The reality is that [Canada has the Atlantic, the Arctic,
and Pacific]{.underline} oceans [to deal with]{.underline} in terms of
security challenges, and [the Arctic]{.underline} Ocean [is going to
increasingly be an area of geopolitical competition as climate change
opens up the sea lines of communication]{.underline} not only to
shipping but also to increased Chinese and Russian assertive behavior as
they aim to secure and dominate the natural resources that exist in that
region. Although more populous than Australia, [Canada]{.underline},
surrounded by three oceans, [is **financially limited** in terms of how
much]{.underline} resources [it can provide for a military
footprint]{.underline} in the Indo-Pacific region. Consequently, this
chapter advocates a capabilities- based approach to engage within the
region and primarily geographically locating that in the eastern part of
the Indo-Pacific, along the Sea of Japan, East China Sea, Taiwan Strait,
and South China Sea, the primary zones of economic interests of Canada
within the broader Indo-Pacific region. Plugging into the Quadrilateral
Security Dialogue or other agreements is an important example of how
Canada can add value to the region within the context of its limited
capacities (Nagy 2021).

### Canada Fails\-\--Nuclear Power

#### Canadian nuclear program is bad

**Spencer-Churchill 25** -- (Julian Spencer-Churchill, 05/16/2025,
associate professor of international relations at Concordia University,
director of the Canadian Centre for Strategic Studies and former
Canadian Forces captain., "Bombs away: A Canadian nuclear weapons
program to deter Trump is not feasible", Policy Options Politiques,
https://policyoptions.irpp.org/magazines/may-2025/canadian-nukes/)

However[, there is no possible way from a political, strategic or
technical perspective to develop a successful Canadian
nuclear]{.underline} deterrent [without creating other serious domestic
and international problems. In fact, such a move would almost certainly
backfire.]{.underline}

We have the necessary resources

Canada has an abundance of the necessary inputs, including 15 per cent
of global production of uranium-238, as well as lithium, beryllium,
lead, graphite and 19 CANDU reactors at three sites (which uniquely can
be refuelled without shutting off the fission process, thus facilitating
the measured production of plutonium-239).

Most of the required mining, refining, milling and waste management take
place in northern Saskatchewan, while there are research facilities in
six of the 10 provinces.

Marc Garneau, the former transport minister and technology champion,
once gave a presentation to my class on Canada's endo- and
exo-atmospheric rocketry, which is mostly designed for meteorological
research but which also demonstrates engineering competence.

Too easy to detect

However[, there is no chance of hiding a nuclear]{.underline} weapons
[program.]{.underline}

[Manufacturing the infrastructure to produce plutonium or to enrich
uranium would be immediately noticeable to the U.S., as well as the
International Atomic Energy Agency.]{.underline}

[Isotope decay products leading to plutonium create easily detectable
Krypton-85 gas. Enrichment centrifuges emit radiofrequency waves that
are difficult to shield. All other enrichment methods either consume
noticeably large quantities of energy or rely on specialized equipment,
such as calutrons.]{.underline}

Although South Africa employed fewer than 20 engineers to manufacture
its six warheads within a large dual-use infrastructure, its attempted
test was immediately detected by satellites of the U.S.S.R. In addition,
Pretoria's purchase of Israeli intermediate-range ballistic missiles was
quickly known by U.S. intelligence.

The nuclear dilemma

There are some other important truths to consider.

First, second-strike nuclear arsenals are the basis of military and
national power, and the U.S. wields great influence through the
provision of its nuclear umbrella to its allies.

While nuclear weapons cannot occupy territory like an army can, they can
shatter the economy of the great powers sufficiently to expose them to
conquest by their neighbours.

A United States de-urbanized and de-industrialized by a major
city-busting Russian or Chinese nuclear strike would be unable to
replace or maintain its navy, which is so vital to assert its sphere of
influence over North America. Conventional forces permit more
fine-grained applications of deterrence and force, but integration with
tactical nuclear forces is doctrinal and theoretical, never having been
tested in battle.

Second, U.S. nuclear power does not depend solely on retaliatory
capability, which is termed immediate deterrence, but also on having an
arsenal so large that potential adversaries do not even try to start a
nuclear program, which is called general deterrence.

[If Canada were to seek nuclear]{.underline} weapons [capability, it
would undermine the psychological deterrent effect of a large U.S.
arsenal and could even lead to nuclear programs in Brazil, Argentina or
Mexico, or a purchased Venezuelan or Cuban arsenal.]{.underline}

Be careful what you wish for

Trump's 51st state threats reveal that Canada has an immature national
security culture. Much like the Liberal government's bewildered response
to the Quebec referendum in 1995, Canadian political leaders lack a
clearly articulated survival strategy for the nation when under stress.

Much recent anti-Americanism in Canada is driven most by economic
concerns related to tariff negotiations, highlighting the lack of a
sense of proportion of many Canadians.

It is because of centuries of harsh historical experience that Russian,
Chinese, French and Polish citizens, for all their jingoism, are more
confidently calm when confronting security threats and war.

Grade school history texts celebrate how stalwart Canadians resisted the
U.S. invasion in the War of 1812, but Canada was mainly lucky that the
U.S. was divided over the issue of war. Alfred Thayer Mahan's regarded
account shows how anglophile New England critically sabotaged the U.S.
war effort, with Boston grain merchants clandestinely feeding the Royal
Navy ships blockading the port of Boston.

A losing proposition

[A formal Canadian nuclear program, designed to assure its citizens,
would instead excite anti-Americanism to new heights and would likely
provoke a physical and irreversible U.S. intervention.]{.underline}

The manifest lack of political will in Ottawa means that politicians
could not credibly threaten retaliation against a U.S. incursion,
defeating the purpose of a nuclear arsenal in the first place.

Any weaponized nuclear program would also require a dramatically
militarized security regime that would disproportionately undermine
Canadian freedoms.

Even possession of a latent pre-assembly capability would make Canada an
easy target of nuclear espionage and fillip to nuclear proliferation.

It was low confidence in Canada's ability to protect from espionage the
design plans of the infamous CF-105 Avro Arrow that led to its
shredding.

International issues would arise

[The foreign relations implications of a Canadian nuclear]{.underline}
weapons [program raise other important questions.]{.underline}

Would Canadian armed forces that are deployed abroad extend our nuclear
umbrella to host nations, such as Latvia?

Where would Canada deploy nuclear weapons? Hudson Bay provides a vast
and easily secured ballistic missile submarine bastion. But at only 100
metres deep, submarines would be vulnerable to detection by
wake-analyzing satellites and air drones equipped with magnetic anomaly
detectors (MAD).

Washington would not appreciate nuclear-armed submarines patrolling in
collision-prone Lake Ontario. Harsh winters might cause missile-carrying
TEL trucks to slide into ditches along an inadequately extensive highway
system.

Would CN and CPKC railway unions demand protocols to minimize the
exposure of their employees if Canada adopted a rail-mobile option,
especially because much of the rail network goes through inhabited
areas?

Deploying first-generation ballistic missile submarines into the deep
oceans would require a protective surface flotilla, exposing its
location.

[Washington wants Canada nuclear free]{.underline}

## Canada CP\-\--NEG

### Say Yes\-\--2NC

#### Canada's a [vital partner]{.underline}.

**Gowling '25** \[Jordan; Updated June 12; Financial Post, "Trump keeps
saying the U.S. doesn't need Canada's stuff. We asked experts if he's
right,"
https://financialpost.com/news/economy/trump-us-doesnt-need-canada-stuff-right\]

Automobiles

[Trump made it clear during his meeting with Carney that the U.S. does
not want **Canadian-made vehicles**]{.underline}. "We want to make our
own cars, we don't really want cars from Canada," said Trump. "At a
certain point, it won't make economic sense for Canada to build those
cars."

[But North America's car industry is one of the most **integrated** in
the **world**]{.underline}, [originating with the **Auto
Pact**]{.underline} that was signed by the U.S. and Canada in 1965.

In 2024, Canada sent nearly 1.1 million vehicles to the U.S. and the
U.S. exported nearly 630,000 vehicles to Canada, according to the Bank
of Montreal.

Charles Bernard, lead economist at the Canadian Automobile Dealers
Association, [said it will take **billions** of **dollars** in
investment and **15 to 20 years**]{.underline} for the American auto
industry to replace the supply of vehicles it imports from Canada and
Mexico.

"[The tariff effects are certainly going to be negative for these
**companies**]{.underline}, [but **moving back** production would be
even **more costly**]{.underline}," said Bernard. "He (Trump) paints a
great image for the Americans about an industry that kind of made the
nation and bringing it back, but there are structural elements that
don't make it a possibility."

However, Bernard said Canada should be concerned about future
investments in the industry if the country is no longer seen as having
reliable tariff-free access to the U.S. market.

Auto parts

Like automobiles, [auto parts were supposed to be subject to **a 25 per
cent tariff**]{.underline}, but the White House confirmed on May 1 that
[Canadian parts made in compliance with the]{.underline} Canada--United
States--Mexico Agreement (**[CUSMA]{.underline}**) [would be
**exempted**]{.underline}.

Bernard said this decision was made following pressure from the U.S.
auto companies that warned the tariffs on auto parts would lead to
higher production costs and higher prices for vehicles.

"[The industrial tissue that links these **three countries**, you can't
necessarily **break them** and **recreate** it in **America right
away**]{.underline}," said Bernard. "Not only will it have a direct
impact on your ability to produce cars, but the cost would be extremely
high."

According to the Canadian Chamber of Commerce Business Lab, [Canada and
Mexico account for **58 per cent of** U.S. auto parts
imports]{.underline}. It is also estimated that [car parts cross the
Canada--U.S. border **six times** on average before **final
assembly**]{.underline}.

"You can't recreate that same optimization in four years," said Bernard.
"You would need to readjust your supply, there would be suppliers in
Canada that they wouldn't find in the U.S., because they don't have the
expertise."

Lumber

Trump has said the U.S. does not need Canadian lumber and, on March 1,
he signed an executive order to accelerate U.S. domestic production of
timber, lumber, paper and other forest products by deregulating logging
on federal lands.

The Canada--U.S. softwood lumber dispute goes back decades, with
combined countervailing and anti-dumping duties on Canadian lumber
currently sitting at just over 14 per cent. These levies are expected to
go up to 34 per cent this fall. Trump has threatened to impose
additional tariffs on top of the duties already in place but so far has
not done so.

[This is because the U.S. **relies heavily** on Canadian **lumber** and
**paper pulp products**]{.underline}. [Canada supplies **24 per cent**
of the U.S.'s **softwood lumber**]{.underline}, [which will be **hard**
to **replace**]{.underline}.

Ian Dunn, president and chief executive of the Ontario Forest Industries
Association, [said it would take **five to 10 years** for the U.S. to
replace the **Canadian market share**]{.underline}.

"They would have to build new capacity, and they would have to build new
mills," said Dunn. "A lot of mills in the U.S. south and pacific
northwest, have shut down or curtailed in the last 16 to 18 months."

[Canada is also a large source of **paper pulp**]{.underline}, a key
input for thousands of different products including paper towels and
toilet paper. [Canada produces **one-third** of the world's **northern
bleached softwood kraft** **pulp** and 75 per cent of **total capacity**
in **North America**]{.underline}.

"Big U.S. companies that supply (paper towel to) Walmart and Costco
really need that northern bleached softwood kraft," said Dunn.

Crude oil

[Canada is the biggest source of **crude oil** for]{.underline} the
U.S., [exporting four **million barrels** of the product per
day]{.underline}. About 90 per cent of Canada's oil exports go to the
U.S., mainly shipped through pipelines from Alberta to American
refineries and south to the U.S. gulf coast.

[Rory Johnston]{.underline}, [an oil market researcher and former
economist at the **Bank of Nova Scotia**]{.underline}, [said the
Canadian and American **oil trade**]{.underline} "[is arguably the two
most **integrated petroleum** systems on the **planet**]{.underline}."

"The whole flow --- so you have quality, you have geography, and you
have pipelines, which basically exclude a functional capability for the
United States to completely displace Canadian oil," said Johnston. "And
even if (they) could, it would take a decade or more and hundreds of
billions of dollars."

The costly process would include the U.S. having to retrofit refineries,
build new pipelines and reverse existing pipelines --- which Johnston
calls a "gigantic headache."

[He added that U.S. oil production has slowed for
**decades**]{.underline} and the recent drop in demand and prices due to
trade headwinds could lead to production going into reverse.

"At those prices, we're likely going to stall out on U.S. crude
production growth already, if not begin to contract outright," said
Johnston. "So, the opposite of drill baby drill."

Despite Trump's rhetoric, crude oil is currently exempt under CUSMA.

Steel and aluminum

Aside from autos, steel and aluminum are the only goods on which Trump
has applied 25 per cent sector-specific tariffs. Trump's issue with
Canadian steel and aluminum has carried over from his first term, when
he imposed Section 232 tariffs on Canadian metals in 2018.

"If we make it in the United States, we don't need it to be made in
Canada," said Trump in early February.

Despite the imposition of tariffs in 2018, [U.S. production capacity of
steel and aluminum did not **meaningfully increase**]{.underline},
according to the Royal Bank of Canada. Overall, RBC said Canada's
[exports of steel and aluminum to the U.S. have increased **by 35 per
cent**]{.underline} to US\$17.7 billion since 2018.

[Canadian steel supports important U.S. industries like
**defence**]{.underline}, **[manufacturing]{.underline}** [and
**shipbuilding**]{.underline}. [The North American steel market is also
**highly integrated**]{.underline}, which allows it to better compete in
the global market, which is subject to major overcapacity issues driven
by China.

Canadian aluminum and steel are also important for the U.S. auto
industry, which can source the two metals in a cost-effective way.
Bernard said nearly 60 per cent of aluminum used in U.S.-made cars is
from Quebec.

"That's a great example of the American president and his team
understanding that it's such a high amount of connected tissue, they
can't start tariffing every layer of it," said Bernard. "Because that
would mean layoffs right away for companies."

The Canadian aluminum sector can also produce more than its U.S.
counterpart, because Canada has more access to electricity. [The U.S.
simply does not have enough **electricity capacity** to power the number
of **aluminum smelters** needed to meet **domestic
demand**]{.underline}, according to the Aluminum Association of Canada.

Potash

[Potash, a key nutrient for plants and input in fertilizers, is
essential for **agriculture worldwide**]{.underline}.

[Canada leads the world in exports of **potash**]{.underline},
[accounting for nearly 41 per cent of all **global trade** of the
product]{.underline}, according to Natural Resources Canada. The only
other countries that rival Canada's output are Russia and Belarus at 18
per cent and 16.6 per cent respectively.

The U.S. is the major destination for Canadian potash, with Canada
supplying nearly 80 per cent of its needs.

"We're the largest producer of potash in the world," said Pierre
Gratton, president and chief executive of the Mining Association of
Canada. "So, it's no surprise they are heavily reliant on our potash."

In response to criticism over what a tariff on potash would mean for
American farmers, Trump lowered the tax to 10 per cent, before
eventually announcing CUSMA exemptions for Canadian goods, including
potash.

[U.S. production of potash accounts for just **one per cent** of
**demand**]{.underline}, according to the Fertilizer Institute. The
Russian and Belarusian potash industries also face challenges after the
U.S. imposed trade sanctions on the two countries after Russia's
invasion of Ukraine in 2022. Recently, Russia and Belarus have also
floated the possibility of export controls on potash, to secure domestic
supply and drive up market prices of the commodity. Any hopes of
replacing Canadian supply in the near-term remain impropable for the
U.S.

#### Canadian and US relations are repairing now -- Gordie Howe International Bridge will attraact further investment and remedy current trade

**Guerrieri 25** \[Vince Guerrieri; Youngstown native with a degree in
journalism from Bowling Green State University. He was a reporter and
sports copy editor for the Pittsburgh Tribune-Review before becoming the
sports editor of the News-Messenger in Fremont in 2005; 7-23-2025; Can
This Bridge Ease the Troubled US-Canadian Relationship?; Bloomberg;
https://www.bloomberg.com/news/features/2025-07-24/detroit-s-gordie-howe-bridge-looks-to-reach-across-us-canada-rift?srnd=homepage-americas;
Accessed 7-24-2025\] HS 宋

The [[new]{.mark} [Gordie Howe International Bridge]{.mark}]{.underline}
is less than two miles long, but it's been a long road to get there.
Plans are on [target for the [opening]{.mark} [this
fall]{.mark}]{.underline} of the six-lane span crossing the Detroit
River between Windsor, Ontario, and Detroit. The bridge, named for a
Canadian hockey great who found fame on the ice for the Detroit Red
Wings, promises to deliver "unparalleled benefits for supply chains,
industry and commercial users," according to the Windsor-Detroit Bridge
Authority, the Canadian crown corporation overseeing construction of the
bridge. "It's far overdue," says Colin Bird, consul general of Canada in
Detroit. "We've been talking about this for the better part of two
decades, but [we're **[five steps away]{.mark}** from the finish
line]{.underline}." It will be the longest cable-stayed bridge in North
America upon its completion, and a feather in the cap of business
boosters on both shores. "[So many of us on this side of the border ---
either us personally or the organizations we represent --- have fought
to get the Gordie Howe Bridge]{.underline}," says Sandy Baruah,
president of the Detroit Regional Chamber. But while it was conceived as
a [[means]{.mark} [of]{.mark} **[strengthening]{.mark} the
[bond]{.mark}** [between]{.mark} the **[US]{.mark}** [and
**Canada**]{.mark}]{.underline}, the Gordie Howe Bridge is arriving at
an uncharacteristically acrimonious moment in American-Canadian
relations. Since taking office in January, President Donald Trump has
lobbed a series of rhetorical and policy attacks on the US's northern
neighbor, announcing punishing tariffs on Canadian goods and threatening
annexation to make it the 51st state. In response, Canadian exports to
the US have fallen off sharply. So has travel to US destinations. The
trade war has clouded the immediate economic prospects for the C\$6.4
billion (\$4.7 billion) structure, which is being built entirely with
Canadian funding and is designed to shore up a vulnerable link in the
supply chain for the Great Lakes region --- a hub for auto manufacturing
on both sides of the border. "[We're finally seeing this needed
reinvestment, which I think is telling a story that we're open for
**business** again]{.underline}." Bird says. "But this progress can be
completely disrupted by a tariff policy that doesn't take into account
the Midwest realities." Breaking a Bottleneck For almost a century,
carmakers and other manufacturers have been obliged to ship their goods
between Windsor and Detroit on the Ambassador Bridge, which has more
crossings annually than the next two busiest cross-border bridges
combined --- the Peace Bridge in Buffalo, New York, and the Blue Water
Bridge in Port Huron, Michigan. (There is also a tunnel between Detroit
and Windsor, publicly owned and privately operated, but that's more for
commuter traffic than commercial traffic.) [[Roughly]{.mark} a
**[quarter]{.mark} [of]{.mark} all [trade]{.mark}** between the US and
Canada [rolls across]{.mark} the aging [four-lane structure]{.mark},
worth [more than **\$100 billion**]{.mark} annually.]{.underline} New
US--Canada Border Crossing The Gordie Howe International Bridge is set
to open later in 2025 [With a name selected to evoke the **peaceable**
relationship between the United States and Canada]{.underline}, the
Ambassador Bridge was the longest suspension bridge in the world when it
opened in 1929 (the George Washington Bridge in New York City supplanted
it two years later). And it was privately constructed. Neither Detroit
nor Windsor had an appetite for building a new bridge, but Detroit's
auto industry saw the demand and funded the span privately. The mayor of
Detroit at the time, John Smith, tried to block the project, seeing the
potential danger of a privately owned border crossing; a referendum in
favor of the bridge passed by an 8-1 margin in a 1927 special election.
"I bow to the will of the people," Smith said. "They want a bridge and
they must have a bridge." But the Ambassador had no sooner opened than
the Great Depression hit. Debt-leveraged to the hilt, the company that
owned it restructured in 1939 and became publicly traded. In 1979, local
trucking magnate Manuel "Matty" Moroun swept in and became majority
owner. For decades afterward, Moroun --- a controversial and
publicity-shy billionaire who died in 2020 --- maintained an iron grip
over the crossing, fighting US and Canadian efforts to construct a
publicly owned rival. Demands for a second bridge became louder
following the terrorist attacks in the US on September 11, 2001, as
tighter security led to longer delays. More recently, the need for
another crossing was vividly demonstrated in 2022, when trucker protests
against the Covid-19 vaccine snarled traffic for miles in Windsor.
"There is a lack of redundancy in the system, which has been a challenge
for the growth of the region," Bird says. ["The [bridge]{.mark} is a
potential [concern as]{.mark} a **[chokepoint]{.mark}**."]{.underline}
Moroun pitched "twinning" the Ambassador Bridge --- building a new
six-lane span next to the current bridge, which he would also control.
In the teeth of the Great Recession, the Michigan legislature tried to
pass a bill for a 50-50 split on costs of its own new bridge.
Eventually, Canada agreed to pay for the construction in full ---
including the toll and inspection facilities on the US side. Moroun
sought to block the project, mounting a campaign against the state
referendum to approve construction and buying an ad on Fox News in 2018
urging President Trump to scuttle the project. Trump did not take up the
cause, and in October of that year, construction on the Gordie
officially started. (The Moroun-owned Detroit International Bridge
Company, which still operates the Ambassador Bridge, did not respond to
requests for comment.) Located about three miles south of the
Ambassador, the Gordie Howe Bridge stands to be a major advance on its
older counterpart. The sleek cable-stayed structure, designed by the
global architecture giant AECOM, boasts 722-foot-tall towers that are
supposed to "reflect the curvature of a hockey stick in a slap shot."
Most importantly, it will be a lot easier to reach by highway. Trucks
crossing into Detroit now need to traverse neighborhoods in Windsor ---
there are eight stoplights from Ontario's busiest highway, the 401, to
the Ambassador Bridge, Bird says. The Gordie Howe Bridge will be a
direct link from US Interstate 75 with the 401. "Freeway-to-freeway
connection is something this area has long needed," Bird says. That
connectivity will be a boon for the automakers who stand to be the
span's biggest power users. Bird estimated that vehicles assembled in
Canada or the Great Lakes region of the United States typically cross
the Detroit River six or seven times. (Indeed, one of the reasons the
Ambassador Bridge became such an important crossing was the Automotive
Products Trade Act of 1965 that removed duties on most auto components
that passed between the two countries.) "When you're doing just-in-time
processing, you can't have the uncertainty of downtown Windsor and
downtown Detroit traffic," Baruah says. Tourists Wanted At the moment,
bridge traffic is less of an issue: Car and truck crossings on the
Ambassador Bridge have plunged dramatically since January, as Canadians
forgo discretionary trips to the US and automakers pull back on exports.
It's a trend that's expected to continue through the summer travel
season. Though Canadian tourism to the US is currently on ice, there's
hope that the Gordie Howe Bridge will eventually draw crowds, and even
become an attraction in and of itself. (It might not hurt that the
bridge's pugnacious namesake also inspired the rallying cry of Canadian
resistance to Trump's tariff threats: "elbows up!") Should daytrippers
arrive, they'll find that, in addition to six lanes for auto and truck
travel, the [span includes a toll-free multiuse path nearly 12 feet wide
for walkers and cyclists]{.underline}. The path will lead to extensive
trail networks on both sides of the border. In Windsor, it will link to
the Great Lakes Waterfront Trail, which itself connects to the
Trans-Canada Trail. On the American side, it connects to the network of
parks and trails on Detroit's riverfront, about 5.5 miles from the
bridge to Belle Isle, a popular Michigan tourist destination. And
downtown Detroit is just about halfway between the Gordie Howe Bridge
and the Belle Isle bridge. Gordon Orr, the chief executive officer of
Tourism Windsor Essex Pelee Island, notes that the bike path wasn't
originally included in plans for the bridge. Rather, Orr said it was
added after Canadian stakeholder meetings with the bridge authority
revealed a desire for it. "I think a lot of people are looking for
unique experiences, and some of them do cross bridges as kind of a
bucket list item," he says. "There will be memories and moments there. I
think there will be wedding proposals on it." Detroit's Baruah said the
uncertainty swirling around the tariff situation makes any predictions
on immediate local impact difficult. "We'll need at least a year of data
and a stable policy environment," he says. [But the [bridge]{.mark} has
been [needed]{.mark} [for]{.mark} a [long]{.mark} [time]{.mark}, he
notes, and it should be [viewed as]{.mark} a **[long-term
solution]{.mark}**. "You're playing a long game]{.underline}," he says.
"[We are exceptionally **[unconcerned]{.mark}** [about]{.mark} the
[**political moment** of the day]{.mark} [and]{.mark} [how]{.mark}
[it]{.mark} might [impact]{.mark} the [utility]{.mark} of the
bridge.]{.underline} The Gordie Howe Bridge is a 100-year investment,
not a four-year investment." Bird is equally confident that the value of
the completed span will stretch well beyond the current political rift
between the two countries. ["It [will]{.mark} [attract **further
investment**]{.mark}," Bird says of the bridge. "As long as we get the
rest of the policy picture right."]{.underline}

#### Canada will say yes to cooperating with US -- given best deal AND only signs of goodwill can disentangle US-dependence.

**Alschner '7-24** \[Wolfgang Alschner; Faculty member at the Centre for
Law, Technology and Society, and an Associate Professor within the
Faculty of Law, Common Law Section at the University of Ottawa;
7-24-2925; Opinion: Canada, we've already got Trump's best trade deal;
