aggregate of multiple interlocking, but independent]{.underline} (or at
least nonlinear and uncoordinated), [variables]{.underline}. In Indian
land tenure, these challenges are particularly acute. Not two but
**[three separate sovereigns]{.underline}**\--**[tribal, state, and
federal]{.underline}**\--**[converge]{.underline}** in reservation
spaces, and [they create three related, but **fundamentally distinct**,
interlocking **legal systems** in which]{.underline} a multitude of
individual [landowners unpredictably operate]{.underline}. 63

Today, there is no such thing as a single \"reservation property\"
form\--rather, [there are]{.underline} three jurisdictionally discrete
land tenure statuses: [individual Indian trust, tribal trust, and fee
properties]{.underline}. 64 [Each]{.underline} tenure status
[incorporates]{.underline} a [different]{.underline} set of [rights and
responsibilities]{.underline} for owners, and these rules are set by a
mix of federal, state, and tribal jurisdictions. 65

Trust property can only be owned by Indians. 66 [Trust properties are
governed largely by the federal government, with some tribal
role]{.underline}. 67 [There are]{.underline} often [different rules
depending on whether the land is in \"tribal trust\" or \"individual
trust.\"]{.underline} 68 Importantly, Indian trust land passes out of
trust and \[\*500\] into fee status whenever it passes to a non-Indian
owner. 69 There are limits on when land can pass to non-Indians in this
way, 70 but when it does, the trust-to-fee conversion is automatic. 71

Fee status property within the reservation is, as a general matter,
categorically outside of the bureaucratic federal land-management system
for trust properties. This has advantages for landowner autonomy and
flexibility, but fee properties are also subject to state property
taxation, while trust properties are not. Fee properties are also
subject to a more unpredictable mix of state and tribal jurisdictions.
72 Fee properties within reservation boundaries can be owned by either
Indians or non-Indians. Indian-owned fee properties are typically
governed by the tribe, but are also often subject to state taxation and
an uncertain mix of other state and local controls, depending on the
exact identity of the owner 73 and where the property is located
(including the character of the surrounding reservation area). 74
Non-Indians cannot own reservation land in trust status but do own fee
lands within reservations. 75 These non-Indian fee landowners are not
citizens of tribal governments, which has complicated tribal assertions
of jurisdiction \[\*501\] over them. Therefore, non-Indian fee lands may
be subject to state or local authorities, and, only to a lesser degree,
tribal control. 76

[A **central challenge**]{.underline} of Indian law [is]{.underline}
that [non-Indian reservation landowners are not politically enfranchised
in tribal governments. Tribal governments]{.underline} possess their own
inherent sovereignty that predates contact with European settlers, 77
but, today, they [are subject to a federal plenary
sovereignty]{.underline}. 78 Originally, government-to-government
treaties between Indian nations and the new U.S. government promised
federal protection for indigenous nations\' exclusive rights to use,
control, and govern all lands within their reserved territories. 79 [The
federal allotment policy\'s pursuit of individual Indians\' assimilation
through private landownership]{.underline}, however, also **[transferred
significant Indian lands to non-Indians in fee status]{.underline}**.
The original purpose of introducing **[non-Indian fee
lands]{.underline}** in this way was to [**facilitate the termination of
tribes as tribes**, and]{.underline}, today\--[as a matter of federal
law]{.underline}\--[individuals who are not biologically
\"Indian\"]{.underline} (and, therefore, do not inherit the attendant
political rights) generally [cannot be recognized as citizens of the
tribal government]{.underline}. 80

Modern [concern about tribal authority]{.underline} over non-Indians,
even when they elect to enter tribal territories, [is rooted in this
**democratic-deficit problem**]{.underline}. 81 The Supreme Court has
expressed significant skepticism about subjecting non-Indians to tribal
laws that are foreign to them. 82 The Court has also emphasized that
Indian nations are \"outside the basic structure of the \[\*502\]
Constitution.\" 83 [Tribal law may]{.underline}, and often does, [treat
non-Indians fairly]{.underline}, 84 [but there is **no federal cause of
action** or waiver of tribal sovereign immunity that guarantees
non-Indians rights comparable to]{.underline} the [constitutional
ones]{.underline} protected by a U.S. court. 85

[The resulting owner-based]{.underline} (rather than territory-based)
[system of property sovereignty **makes land tenure more complex** in
Indian country]{.underline}. 86 [Trust and fee land owners
are]{.underline} often [subject to **different jurisdictions** and have
**vastly different property rights** depending on their tenure status,
even if their lands are immediately adjacent]{.underline} within one
reservation.

[This]{.underline} system [turns the typical relationship between
property and sovereignty on its head]{.underline}. Generally,
sovereignty encompasses dominion over territory, and part of a
sovereign\'s right and responsibility is to define, and then protect,
the institution of private property ownership for all persons and land
within that territorial domain. 87 In Indian country, [who governs
where]{.underline} often [turns **not on**]{.underline} the territorial
**[location]{.underline}** of the property, **[but]{.underline}**,
rather, on **[the identity of the owners, creating a
property-by-property]{.underline}** (or interest-by-interest)
**[jurisdictional mix-and-match that is disjointed, difficult, and
costly to operate]{.underline}**. 88 [Individual landowner
decisions]{.underline}\--[whether to transfer to an Indian or a
non-Indian]{.underline}, for example, [or whether to hold in fee or in
trust]{.underline}\--[change jurisdictional dynamics at the individual
parcel level]{.underline}. In addition, [whole neighborhood demographics
and characteristics]{.underline}\--whether an area is predominantly
Indian or not\--[can]{.underline} also [shape wider regulatory authority
issues]{.underline}, such as which sovereign asserts zoning authority
where. 89

Moreover, the jurisdictional differentiation is even more complicated
than this construct of a property-by-property jurisdictional
checkerboard \[\*503\] suggests. 90 [Co-ownership]{.underline}, or
fractionation, [is **pervasive on Indian trust lands**]{.underline}. 91
Many of these [fractionated trust lands have]{.underline} \"**[tens or
hundreds of different owners]{.underline}**, all **[with varying levels
of interests]{.underline}**.\" 92 [Co-owners each possess small, but
undivided, shares of the whole parcel]{.underline}. 93 Today, many of
these jointly owned tracts are actually in a mixed tenure or
\"emulsified\" state, with some of the undivided interests held in fee,
and other undivided interests in the same property in individual and
tribal trust statuses. 94 These [mixed-tenure, emulsified properties
**exacerbate jurisdictional issues immensely** as governance is
differentiated in even more **finely grained** and
**difficult-to-distinguish ways**]{.underline}. These
[undivided-property co-owners]{.underline} often [have **completely
incongruent property rights** to the same resource]{.underline}. In
terms of overall complexity, in many scenarios jurisdiction is not only
differentiated parcel by parcel, but co-owner by co-owner (or subject
matter by subject matter, or right by right) within single tracts. 95

#### High barrier to entry makes navigating the process impossible. 

Jessica A. **Shoemaker 17**, Assistant Professor of Law at the
University of Nebraska College of Law, J.D. from the University of
Wisconsin Law School, "Complexity's Shadow: American Indian Property,
Sovereignty, and the Future," Michigan Law Review, Vol. 115, February
2017, accessed 07-23-2025, Nexis Uni, DG

[Technicality is]{.underline} \"[a function of the fineness of the
distinctions a rule makes]{.underline}, the [specialized
terminology]{.underline} it employs, [and]{.underline} the [refined
substantive judgments it requires]{.underline}.\" 96 In this sense,
technicality is relative to the audience expected to interpret and
implement the rule. 97 [In Indian land tenure, landowners, their
advocates, and the government itself bear the cost of a **highly
technical legal framework**. Indian law]{.underline} is always postured
as the exception to \"regular\" rules. 98 It [incorporates a long and
vast, specialized legal history]{.underline} \[\*504\] that is [**not
easily accessible** to a spontaneous observer. In land tenure
specifically]{.underline}, many [property law terms have unique meanings
that depart from conventional understandings, which]{.underline} further
[**complicates engagement** by]{.underline} both [landowners and legal
advocates]{.underline}.

To start, many [law students complete an]{.underline} entire juris
doctor [degree with]{.underline} little or [no mention of]{.underline}
the tribal sovereign or [federal Indian law]{.underline}. Even the most
basic operative terms in Indian law\--defining Indian versus non-Indian,
understanding the difference between trust tenure status and fee
status\--are loaded with histories and multifaceted meanings that are
not readily accessible. For example, [the definition of]{.underline}
\"[Indian]{.underline}\" [**varies** according to the subject area, law,
and purpose]{.underline} for which it is being deployed. Indian [status
is defined with reference to both federal and tribal law]{.underline},
and [it incorporates]{.underline} a range of [nuanced,
individual-specific factors]{.underline}. 99 On top of this are a series
of other critical, but highly specialized relevant legal questions,
including whether and how Public Law 280 affects state and federal
jurisdiction in some, but not all, Indian reservations; 100 how the
Indian Reorganization Act may or may not uniquely affect alienation of
some lands within a given tribe\'s territory; 101 and the relevance of a
tribe\'s particular treaty history. 102 [There is also]{.underline} the
challenge of [**simply being able to decipher what an Indian landowner
owns**, which requires translation of a **federal inventory of an
individual\'s trust assets**]{.underline}\--**[a cryptic, mostly
numerical record of ownership]{.underline}**. 103 [A non-specialist is
unlikely to know enough to begin to ask these questions]{.underline},
and [even a specialist will find these distinctions and judgments
difficult to make]{.underline}.

In addition, [Indian land tenure introduces a]{.underline} whole [series
of **unique property law** twists that apply only in this
field]{.underline}. As just one example, Congress relatively recently
passed the highly technical American Indian Probate \[\*505\] Reform Act
of 2004 (AIPRA). 104 [AIPRA governs disposition of Indian trust property
upon an Indian landowner\'s death,]{.underline} whether through
intestate succession or testate devise. 105 But **[the rules are
difficult to understand and apply]{.underline}**. For example, a special
primogeniture, or \"single heir,\" rule applies to prevent intestate
distribution to multiple heirs, but only when the fractional
co-ownership interest to be distributed amounts to less than 5 percent
of the total ownership in the tract. 106 In addition, intestacy
distribution is limited to those who satisfy a detailed rubric of who
qualifies as an \"eligible heir\" and who does not. 107 Eligible heirs
have to be Indian (also specially defined for the purposes of this
statute), 108 \"lineal descendents \[sic\] within 2 degrees of
consanguinity of an Indian\[,\]\" or a co-owner of a trust interest in
the same property. 109 AIPRA also uniquely creates a presumption that
certain language in a will creates a joint tenancy with a right of
survivorship, 110 instead of the typical tenancy-in-common default that
applies in other contexts. Other detailed rules exist\--with multiple
layers of processes for each\--for things like tribal-purchase options
and landowner-consolidation agreements at probate. 111 Tribes are also
authorized, subject to federal approval, to adopt their own tribal
probate codes supplanting AIPRA\'s provisions 112\--requiring Indian
probate practitioners to assess what, if anything, the tribal law
provides, and to which interests it applies, including where an
individual Indian\'s estate may include trust assets located in
different reservations. 113

\[\*506\] The [partition rules]{.underline} under AIPRA [provide another
example. Outside of the Indian land tenure context, partition refers to
the absolute right of every co-owner of real property to exit the
co-ownership]{.underline}, either [by forcing a physical division of the
property or a partition sale with pro rata distribution of sale
proceeds]{.underline}. 114 [AIPRA did authorize a new partition sale
option]{.underline}\--but here, partition means a totally unique process
of executing a particular kind of forced sale [only available on
qualifying \"highly fractionated Indian lands\" after a **detailed
application process**]{.underline}, consent from at least 50 percent of
co-ownership interests in the land, and a mandatory appraisal review.
115 The ultimate sale must take place at an auction with limits on who
qualifies to bid, and with the caveat that the final price at auction
must at least match a previously determined, formal appraised value. 116
**[The process takes a master technician to accomplish]{.underline}**.
117

#### Independently, the federal government can individually disprove of [every project]{.underline}, even if the plan makes it theoretically legal.

Jessica A. **Shoemaker 17**, Assistant Professor of Law at the
University of Nebraska College of Law, J.D. from the University of
Wisconsin Law School, "Complexity's Shadow: American Indian Property,
Sovereignty, and the Future," Michigan Law Review, Vol. 115, February
2017, accessed 07-23-2025, Nexis Uni, DG

As highlighted by the AIPRA discussion above, **[administrative regimes
are]{.underline}**, by definition, \"**[almost inevitably more complex
than legislation]{.underline}**,\" in large part [because
of]{.underline} the proliferation of [numerous and encompassing
regulations]{.underline}. 118 Indian [trust properties are regulated
primarily by the]{.underline} Bureau of Indian Affairs
([BIA]{.underline}) [and also, in some cases, by tribal administrative
agencies, who can contract with the federal government]{.underline} to
perform the BIA\'s role, albeit still subject to federal rules and
authority. 119 These authorities \[\*507\] cover nearly every aspect of
Indian trust property, including not only distribution of assets after
death, but also most inter vivos property rights, including all kinds of
real property transfers, and even an owner\'s ability to possess her own
land.

For example, federal law includes a near-absolute restraint on
alienation on Indian lands that limits any transfer, including
inter-family gifts, credit-securing collateral agreements, and, of
course, any kind of for-value transfers. 120 Under traditional property
law canons, free transferability is fundamental to a functioning
property system. 121 [In Indian trust land]{.underline}, however, [the
federal government **individually approves or disapproves every lease,
mortgage, and transfer**]{.underline}\--often [requiring a full
appraisal and a bureaucratic assessment of whether the transaction is in
the Indian landowner\'s best interests before deciding whether to
approve a request]{.underline}. 122 These alienation restraints may also
apply to a less certain degree to Indian-owned non-trust property. 123

[Use and possession of Indian land is]{.underline} also [comprehensively
regulated, largely by the federal government]{.underline}. Outside of
the Indian law context, any [co-owner of jointly owned land has a direct
right to use and possess her own land, subject only to the parallel
rights of any other co-owners and]{.underline} generally applicable
[land-use regulations]{.underline}. 124 In the Indian lands context,
however, the federal government now oversees even a co-owner\'s own use
of her trust land. 125 Under existing federal rules, Indian co-owners
have no direct right to possess trust properties absent a federally
approved lease or co-owner permission, and Indian owners must pay
fair-market-value rent for their own possession, at least to the other
Indian co-owners, and regardless of \[\*508\] whether these other
co-owners have actually asserted any interest in making use of the land
themselves. 126 In reality, all of an Indian co-owner\'s property rights
are mediated and defined through encompassing federal regulatory
structures. 127

Non-owners who seek to use Indian lands also must navigate each proposed
use through the regulatory process for acquiring consent from Indian
owners (or co-owners) as well as the BIA. Title 25 of the Code of
Federal Regulations is thick with Indian-specific land regulations to
direct this multi-actor process. For example, a proposed user of Indian
trust property would have to consult (and follow) Part 167 for grazing
permits, Part 169 for rights-of-ways over Indian lands, Parts 211 for
tribal mineral development and 212 for individual mineral development,
or any of the many respective subparts in Part 162 for various types of
Indian leases, with special sub-rules for agricultural leases, homesite
leases, business leases, energy leases, and other nonspecific,
nonagricultural leases. 128

In some cases, federal law allows tribal law to supplant federal rules,
but often only after a federal vetting and approval process for the
tribal enactment. 129 These tribal regulations would then apply, but
typically only to the extent they do not conflict with other federal law
or a more amorphous sense of the federal trust responsibility. 130

\[\*509\] On top of all this, [whether the tribe or state exercises
zoning or other land-use authority]{.underline} (from environmental
regulations to building-permit procedures) [is a **separate and
difficult jurisdictional question**]{.underline}. This [shifting
land-use authority can add additional layers of **dense**]{.underline}
(**[and hard-to-read]{.underline}**) **[regulation over reservation land
uses]{.underline}**. 131

### No Solvency\-\--Offense\-\--2NC

#### This is [offense]{.underline}\-\--landowners will [sell]{.underline}, driving [poverty]{.underline}, [homelessness]{.underline}, and [unemployment]{.underline}. 

Jessica A. **Shoemaker 17**, Assistant Professor of Law at the

[Too much complexity can **drive out desirable activity** as
**participants elect to disengage**]{.underline} from the field. 158
[This withdrawal is rational]{.underline}, though socially undesirable,
[when **complexity becomes too costly**]{.underline} for individuals to
bear.

[Property law]{.underline} in particular [**requires stability** and
**clarity** so]{.underline} that [owners know their rights and
responsibilities and can invest in accordance with those]{.underline}
internalized [expectations]{.underline}. 159 Reservation landowners have
no such clarity. The [**jurisdictional complexity adds costs** to any
desirable land use]{.underline}. Simply det[ermining who governs what
is]{.underline} often **[fraught with peril]{.underline}**. 160 On top

of fractionated ownership and the **burdensome**]{.underline}\--but
intermittent\--[**restrictions of the federal land-management regime**.
This]{.underline} complexity [**limits access to capital, directly
impedes flexible land use**, and **encourages disengagement** as **the
costs of active participation exceed the likely benefits**]{.underline}.

At the same time, [communities]{.underline} often [benefit]{.underline}
most [from local landowners making local uses of their
lands]{.underline}. 162 This is missing in many Indian communities. The
large majority of fractionated Indian lands are idle, not generating any
income, or, if they are in use, being leased to non-Indians. 163 Data on
other tenure forms in Indian country are harder to collect, but
\[\*514\] [terrible **poverty, homelessness**, and **unemployment**
persists]{.underline} throughout many reservations, despite widespread
land ownership. 164 **[Landowners\' withdrawal]{.underline}** may be

the wider community level]{.underline}**.

#### This is a reason they lose under their own framing\-\--the land tenure system is [structurally unjust]{.underline}. 

Jessica A. **Shoemaker 17**, Assistant Professor of Law at the
University of Nebraska College of Law, J.D. from the University of
Wisconsin Law School, "Complexity's Shadow: American Indian Property,
Sovereignty, and the Future," Michigan Law Review, Vol. 115, February
2017, accessed 07-23-2025, Nexis Uni, DG

[The]{.underline} predominantly [top-down federal land tenure
regime]{.underline} that persists today [continues to
impact]{.underline} not only Indian landowners, but also **[tribal
sovereignty]{.underline}** in many of the same inflexible ways. Just as
[indigenous nations historically had their own diverse land tenure
systems and property institutions]{.underline}, many Native scholars
continue to emphasize the unique modern relationship with the land that
persists in many American Indian communities today. 300 Before European
contact, indigenous nations had many various land tenure systems. 301
The nomadic tribes where systems were based on shifting hunting
territories typically had temporary use rights. 302 Farming tribes that
\[\*537\] were more settled often had more established guarantees of
individual possession. 303 In many indigenous cultures, however, land
has a fully different concept. 304 Thus, how we define legal rights to
land is critically important\--economically, but also socially and
culturally. 305 Our [systems of land tenure shape]{.underline} many
[other dimensions of human life: distributions of economic entitlements,
access to shelter and spaces for sustenance, and inclusion or exclusion
from community life]{.underline}. 306 In indigenous communities, land
and place are often particularly intertwined with religion and culture
and even identity as a \"people.\" 307 How we understand \"the
land\"\--our land ethic\--also shapes many other decisions. Are we
stewards of a sacred place? Is the land our responsibility to develop?
Are resources fungible commodities we should use before we lose? 308 In
this way, property law communicates social values.

Under this account of Indian land tenure, [the central problem is the
**completely dysfunctional governance systems** that created
this]{.underline} property [regime. Tribes **do not truly communicate
their land ethics or organize social relations** through the mess of
jurisdictional checkerboards, emulsions, and property-versus-sovereignty
stratifications]{.underline}. 309 Instead, [the system perpetuates a
**high degree of federal control** and]{.underline}, in areas of
uncertainty, [**sovereign-to-sovereign animosity** and
**land-use-related jurisdictional conflict**]{.underline}. 310

\[\*538\] One of the founding figures of the progressive property
school, Morris Cohen, argued that modern property rights are delegations
of state sovereign authority to private individuals, who receive rights
good against the world. 311 As such, allocations, distributions, and
definitions of individual property rights must be justified by the same
sense of morality and public good that any direct state action would
require. 312 It is striking how much of Indian land tenure is divorced
from this central tenet. Indian property rights are not allocated to
reflect sovereign values. Rather, [Indian property rights
are]{.underline} somehow [used to allocate jurisdictional rights between
sovereigns. This topsy-turvy reversal of how property and
sovereignty]{.underline} typically [work **does not achieve broad-based
flourishing**, reflective of indigenous social values, in Indian
communities]{.underline}. Even more troubling, [Indian
people]{.underline}, who are [uniquely subject to this perverse system,
are]{.underline} certainly **[not treated as human beings entitled to
the equal dignity and respect they deserve]{.underline}**. 313

#### The 'trust relationship' is rooted in [racist]{.underline}, [paternalistic logic]{.underline} that only aids in subjugating Indigenous people. 

Adam **Crepelle 21**, Associate Professor of Law at Southern University
Law Center, Co-Founder of the American Indian Chamber of Commerce of
Louisiana, Citizen of the United Houma Nation, J.D. from the Southern
University Law Center, LL.M. in Indigenous Peoples Law and Policy from
the University of Arizona James E. Rogers College of Law, "White Tape
and Indian Wards: Removing the Federal Bureaucracy to Empower Tribal
Economies and Self-Government," University of Michigan Journal of Law
Reform, Vol. 54, No. 3, Spring 2021, accessed 07-23-2025, HeinOnline, DG

**[Racism has played a significant role in]{.underline}** United
States-Indian relations, and nowhere is this more obvious than **[the
\"trust relationship.\"]{.underline}** The trust relationship has been
repeatedly recognized by all three branches of the federal
government.147 What the federal government fails to mention is that [the
trust relationship is **overtly predicated on** notions of **Indian
inferiority**]{.underline}.\' In fact, [the trust relationship is little
more than a euphemistic rephrasing of]{.underline} Chief Justice John
[Marshall\'s pronouncement]{.underline} that [the relationship
between]{.underline} Indian [tribes and the **U**nited
**S**tates]{.underline} \"**[resembles that of a ward to his
guardian]{.underline}**.\"149

Contemporary [Indian trust land can be traced to]{.underline} the 1823
decision of [Johnson v. M\'Intosh]{.underline}.150 In this case, the
[Court held]{.underline} that [Indian tribes lost ownership]{.underline}
of their land [when Europeans arrived]{.underline} on the continent;
however, the Indians maintained the right to occupy their land.151 The
Court reached this conclusion [by resorting to]{.underline} the Doctrine
of Discovery,1 a doctrine of [international law that **authorizes the
subjugation of non-Christian Europeans at the hands of Christian
kingdoms**]{.underline}.153 Chief Justice [Marshall stated]{.underline}
that [the Indians\' \"character and religion\" justified the \"superior
genius of Europe\"]{.underline}154 claiming control of all lands in the
Americas. Despite factual errors and patently racist language, Johnson
v. M\'Intosh remains binding precedent.155

The General Allotment Act of 1887 was designed to destroy Indian culture
by erasing Indian reservations and forcing Indians in- to the U.S.
mainstream.156 Under the Act, each head of household received 160 acres
of fee land that was placed in trust for twenty- five years.157 At the
end of the period, [the theory was]{.underline} that [Indians would
become farmers]{.underline} like their white counterparts [and own the
land outright in fee simple]{.underline}.158 None of this came to bear,
and this was no surprise. The legislative history of the Allotment Act
reveals the bill\'s authors intended the law to be a transfer of Indian
lands to whites.159

[Allotment proved]{.underline} to be [an **unmitigated
disaster**]{.underline} for Indians.160 Agricultural implements were
supposed to accompany allotments; however, the United States failed to
deliver on this promise, making it quite difficult for poor Indians to
become farmers.161 Furthermore, the majority of [land]{.underline} the
Indians were allotted [was unsuitable for farming]{.underline}.162 The
valuable land went to whites.163 In total, Indians lost ninety million
acres of their treaty-guaranteed land through the General Allotment
Act.164 A government report published four decades after the Allotment
Act found that all the Act did was cast Indians into tremendous economic
despair.165

Race played a vital role in the United States\' Indian land policy as
elucidated in 1906 legislation. The Clapp Amendment removed land
restrictions on \"mixed blood\" Indians of the White Earth Reservation
but kept \"full blood\" Indians of the White Earth Reservation under
federal wardship until \"the Secretary of the Interior \[was\] satisfied
that said adult full-blood Indians are competent to handle their own
affairs.\"f6 Congress also forbade citizens of the Five Civilized Tribes
who were \"full-blood Indian\" from conducting any land transaction on
their allotments without congressional approval. 167 And, through the
Burke Act, the twenty-five-year trust period could be discarded if the
Indian allottee was deemed \"competent\" as determined by a federal
\"competency commission.\"168 [One of the mechanisms used to measure
competency was **degree of Indian blood**]{.underline}; in fact, the
U.S. Secretary of the Interior adopted a policy that Indians with \"less
than one-half Indian blood\" were presumed to be competent.169
Contrarily, \"all adult Indians of one-half or more Indian blood\" could
only be found competent \"after careful investigation.\"170

Allotment and its assimilationist ideology ended in 1934 with passage of
the Indian Reorganization Act (IRA).171 Allotment was predicated on the
notion that Indian tribes should vanish, whereas the IRA was premised on
the belief that tribes should exist.172 Hence, [the IRA authorized the
restoration and expansion of tribal lands]{.underline}.173 In passing
the IRA, the United States acknowledged that the nation\'s prior Indian
policies had been patronizing and demeaning and had devastatingly
deleterious effects on Indians.174 The Supreme Court has described the
IRA\'s purpose as \"to rehabilitate the Indian\'s economic life and to
give him a chance to develop the initiative destroyed by a century of
oppression and paternalism.\"\" 5 Though the IRA is not without flaws,\"
it is undoubtedly positive legislation for tribes. Nonetheless, a
superabundance of federal regulations remain on tribal lands.\"

The [gobs of land regulations that apply nowhere else but Indian country
are **irrational** and **serve no legitimate purpose**. Trust land came
into existence because Indians were **deemed racially inferior** and
**consequently incompetent to own their land**]{.underline}.\" **[This
bigoted belief remains the cornerstone of Indian trust
land]{.underline}**. \" Today[, Indians are still required to prove
competency]{.underline} in order [to liberate their land from trust
status]{.underline},180 [and degree of Indian blood remains a factor
when an Indian seeks to liberate]{.underline} her [land from the federal
trust relationship]{.underline}.\'\' [The]{.underline} federal
government\'s [application of Indian blood rather than
citizenship]{.underline} in a federally recognized tribe [distinguishes
it from the constitutional tribal citizenship framework]{.underline}.182
Thus, arguably, the restrictions on trust land should be subjected to
strict scrutiny due to their racist origins. Regardless, the [federal
restrictions cannot survive under rational basis review
because]{.underline} the **[bureaucratic constraints on tribal land use
are irrational]{.underline}**.

The federal [restrictions on trust land **incapacitate tribal
economies** and **frustrate** tribal **self-governance**]{.underline}.
Unlike the Indian employment preference that was upheld in Morton v.
Mancari,83 **[federal trust land regulations do not further tribal
self-government]{.underline}**. Rather, [trust land does just the
opposite because trust land **requires tribes to seek federal approval**
for **nearly all activity**]{.underline} on trust land.184 [Federal
overlordship is the **antithesis of Indian
self-government**]{.underline}. The [bureaucratic fetters]{.underline}
that encumber trust land **[thwart virtually all efforts
at]{.underline}** Indian country economic
**[development]{.underline}**.185 [Studies consistently
show]{.underline} that [non-Indian fee land is **significantly more
productive**]{.underline} than adjacent trust land,\' [and]{.underline}
as additional proof of the failure of trust land, [the **U**nited
**S**tates has \"lost\" **billions** of dollars from Indian trust land
accounts.]{.underline}\'\' In the true spirit of inept federal
bureaucracy, the cost of administering trust land is often far more than
the value of the land itself 188 Tribes do not benefit when the federal
government controls their land.

[The federal government is **terrible at managing Indian land and
resources because the U**nited **S**tates **has no incentive to
behave**]{.underline}. Although the trust relationship between federally
recognized tribes and the United States is legally enforceable,189 [the
Supreme Court has gone to tremendous lengths to shield the federal
government from liability for]{.underline} even the most **[extreme
mismanagement]{.underline}** of Indian resources. 190 The Supreme Court
has held that [a tribe **cannot sue** the **U**nited **S**tates when a
federal official engages in overtly corrupt behavior]{.underline} to the
tribe\'s detriment191 [and]{.underline} has also ruled that [tribes
cannot]{.underline} even [access documents to determine whether the
**U**nited **S**tates has mismanaged a tribe\'s
resources]{.underline}.192 Decrying the Supreme Court\'s refusal to hold
the United States accountable for mismanaging tribal resources, Justice
Sotomayor has written, \"[had this]{.underline} type of [mismanagement
taken place in **any other trust arrangements**]{.underline} such as
Social Security, **[there would be war]{.underline}**.\"193

[As a result of]{.underline} the Secretary of the [Interior\'s
consistently **abhorrent supervision of Indian trust land**]{.underline}
and resources, one federal court opined:

> Alas, our \"modern\" [Interior]{.underline} department has time and
> again [demonstrated that it is a **dinosaur**-the morally and
> culturally oblivious hand-me-down of a **disgracefully racist** and
> imperialist **government that should have been buried a century ago**,
> the **last pathetic outpost of the indifference and anglocentrism we
> thought we had left behin**d]{.underline}.194

The federal government\'s [**totalitarian control of Indian trust land
must come to an end**. Land restrictions based upon the
belief]{.underline} that In[dians are incompetent **have no place** in
the twenty-first century]{.underline} United States.195 Plus, [the
federal government has proven itself **profoundly incapable of
administering Indian land**]{.underline}. The current [trust land
regulations are irrational and promote no legitimate government
purpose]{.underline}. The current federal restrictions on Indian trust
land are unconstitutional.

### Trusts Fail\-\--1NC

#### Trusts [fail]{.underline}\-\--economic barriers to development remain post-plan. 

**Alston et al. 24**, Eric Alston, Scholar in Residence in the Finance
Division and the Faculty Director of the Hernando de Soto Capital
Markets Program at the University of Colorado Boulder Leeds School of
Business, M.A. in Economics from the University of Maryland, J.D. from
University of Chicago Law School; Adam Crepelle, Associate Professor of
Law at Southern University Law Center, Co-Founder of the American Indian
Chamber of Commerce of Louisiana, Citizen of the United Houma Nation,
J.D. from the Southern University Law Center, LL.M. in Indigenous
Peoples Law and Policy from the University of Arizona James E. Rogers
College of Law; Wilson Law, Assistant Professor of Economics at Baylor
University, Ph.D. in Economics from the University of Wisconsin-Madison,
Ilia Murtazashvili, Associate Professor at the University of Pittsburgh
Graduate School of Public and International Affairs, Ph.D. in Political
Science from the University of Wisconsin-Madison, "The chronic
uncertainty of American Indian property rights," Journal of
Institutional Economics, Vol. 17, No. 3, June 2021, accessed 07-23-2025,
https://doi.org/10.1017/S1744137420000636, DG

Despite some benefits for tribes, [**trust land comes with significant
burdens** such as **complex** and **burdensome federal regulations**
that make entrepreneurship much more costly]{.underline} on reservations
(Miller et al., 2019). Although tribal land ownership and sovereignty
are currently intertwined, this does not have to be the case as
[non-Indians who purchase land]{.underline} within a reservation [could
easily be subjected to tribal law]{.underline} (EagleWoman, 2009).
[Conditioning which law applies on]{.underline} the [ownership
status]{.underline} of the land [and whether an individual is an Indian
**increases**]{.underline} the [**transaction costs** of]{.underline}
engaging in [productive economic activity]{.underline} using resources
on reservation. Tribal sovereignty is more meaningful than it has been
in the past 200 years (Washburn, 2016: 207); nevertheless, [tribes'
sovereignty over their land and economic activities]{.underline}
occurring on it [remains **aspirational**]{.underline} across a number
of dimensions.

[Constraints]{.underline} on the free determination of property
institutions by tribes [are compounded by rules that impose different
burdens on Indians that the federal government does not apply to other
groups and quasi-sovereign entities]{.underline} in the United States.
One issue that has emerged due to the complexity of the institutional
history on reservation (due to Supreme Court precedent) is that
[different laws apply based upon whether a person is an Indian or
not]{.underline} (Anderson and Parker, 2008). Further [complications
arise]{.underline} as a result of **[dozens of definitions
of]{.underline}** the legal term **['Indian' in federal code, case law,
and tribal codes]{.underline}** (White et al., 2008). Another issue is
that [tribes are precluded from establishing a substantial revenue base.
Although states cannot tax trust land itself, **states can tax
commerce** that occurs on tribal trust land if people other
than]{.underline} citizens of [the tribe are involved]{.underline} in
the transaction. [This]{.underline} effectively [**prohibits tribes from
collecting taxes** because **no one wants to pay** both a tribal and
state tax when the tribal tax can be avoided by stepping off the
reservation]{.underline} (Crepelle, 2020). [The problem is further
compounded by a **lack of business activity**, which undermines tribal
revenue]{.underline}. Thus, even if we view the tribal governments as
subjected to federal oversight like other quasi-sovereign units (such as
state governments), Indian reservations face additional constraints.

A larger institutional challenge is that many [tribes still operate
under **culturally incompatible IRA constitutions** imposed on them by
the federal government]{.underline}. Cornell and Kalt's (1998, 2000)
finding that separation of powers is as important in Indian country as
anywhere else, especially to limit corruption, remains significant. For
example, those tribes that have successfully formed a strong and
independent judiciary have stronger economies (Crepelle, 2019). Even
with removal of federal regulations on Indian economies, [there remain
**political** and **legal challenges** to]{.underline} the creation of
more [effective property institutions]{.underline} on Indian
reservations. [The effectiveness of legal rights depends]{.underline} in
part [on **administrative capacity** and **political
constraints**]{.underline} on sovereign tribal governments, [as well as
judges and lawyers with expertise in Indian law, police who are
accountable for tribal citizens, and meritorious tribal
bureaucrats]{.underline}. Indians may choose private property rights
with greater self-determination. Our analysis suggests that [the
challenges remain of building political and legal autonomy and capacity
to enable greater self-determination of property
institutions]{.underline}. The gains from doing so are potentially
immense. [Entrepreneurial economic actors]{.underline} (whether American
Indian or no) [can be deterred from making productive investments due to
**chronic uncertainty** created by decades of mistreatment]{.underline}
under the extant political economies of the time. But the question
remains whether the predatory state can credibly commit to a durable new
institutional equilibrium on reservation. Our argument would suggest
that [the stability associated with credible state commitment is central
to determining long run economic outcomes]{.underline} (Broadberry and
Wallis, 2017), in addition to the specific institutional challenges that
any given reservation may be facing.

### Trusts Fail\-\--2NC

#### It takes [millions of dollars]{.underline} and [years of work]{.underline} to open a [single business]{.underline}. 

Adam **Crepelle 23**, Assistant Professor of Law at the Loyola
University Chicago School of Law, Co-Founder of the American Indian
Chamber of Commerce of Louisiana, Citizen of the United Houma Nation,
J.D. from the Southern University Law Center, LL.M. in Indigenous
Peoples Law and Policy from the University of Arizona James E. Rogers
College of Law, "It Shouldn\'t Be This Hard: The Law and Economics of
Business in Indian Country," Utah Law Review, Vol. 2023, No. 5, 2023,
accessed 07-24-2025, HeinOnline, DG

Indian country\'s regulatory structure is complex, and this is
particularly true of land tenure. There are three primary types of land
in Indian country\--fee simple, trust lands, and restricted fee lands.
76 The first type, fee simple land,77 is land an individual personally
holds title to which they may freely alienate. 78 Fee simple is the most
common land tenure form in the United States. 79 However, [Indian
country\'s predominant form is]{.underline} the second type: [trust
land]{.underline}.80 [Individuals **cannot own trust
land**]{.underline}; rather, [they can acquire use rights for it while
title remains with the federal government]{.underline}.78 Thus, [trust
land **cannot be conveyed without federal approval**]{.underline}. 82
The final primary type of land in Indian country is restricted fee
land.83 Although an individual Indian or the tribe owns restricted land,
interests in restricted fee land are nontransferable absent federal
approval. 84 Hence, restricted fee land is the functional equivalent of
trust land.85 Even if only a small portion of a tract of land is held in
trust or restricted status, a transaction that predominately involves
fee simple land is unlawful without first gaining the Secretary of the
Interior\'s approval. 86 [Discerning land status can require **lengthy
searches** of treaties, statutes, and other documents]{.underline}. 87
[This takes **time and money** that]{.underline} usually [does not have
to be expended on land outside of Indian country]{.underline}.

[Assuming a business wishes to operate]{.underline} on trust land, the
Bureau of Indian Affairs\' (\"[BIA]{.underline}\") [permission is
required]{.underline}88 [and obtaining]{.underline} the BIA\'s
[permission can take over a year]{.underline}.89 Indeed, [the BIA **must
approve the lease price**]{.underline} of individual Indian trust land90
[as well as the method by which the individual]{.underline} Indian [is
paid]{.underline}.91 [The BIA will not authorize a business lease
without \"\[e\]nvironmental and archeological reports, surveys, and site
assessments]{.underline} as needed to facilitate compliance with
applicable Federal and tribal environmental and land use
requirements.\"92 Relevant federal laws include the National Historic
Preservation Act,93 the Archeological Resources Protection Act,94 the
Native American Graves Protection and Repatriation Act95 and the
National Environmental Policy Act (\"NEPA\"). 96

NEPA is triggered by \"major federal actions.\" 97 Ninety-five percent
of major federal actions receive categorical exclusions, 98 and the
projects can proceed in two days. 99 However, the Environmental
Protection Agency notes, \"[Activities in]{.underline} Indian country
and in other [tribal areas often require a **greater level of NEPA
involvement** than the same activities in non-tribal areas when
these]{.underline} activities [occur on tribal lands held in
trust]{.underline} by the federal government.\"100 This means, [**at
minimum**, an]{.underline} environmental assessment
(\"[EA]{.underline}\") [is usually required]{.underline} in Indian
country.\' 101 According to the Government Accountability Office
(\"GAO\"), [the average cost]{.underline} to complete an EA [is
\$**301,000**]{.underline}102 [and can **easily take six
months**]{.underline}.103 [Many]{.underline} Indian country [projects
will require **full**]{.underline} environmental impact statement
(\"**[EIS]{.underline}**\"). The GAO estimates [the average EIS costs
\$**6.6 million**]{.underline} 104 [and takes **4.6 years to
complete**]{.underline}. 105 [While a business is open and churning a
profit outside of Indian country, the same business in Indian country is
**suffocating in red tape**]{.underline}.106

Indian country land use has the potential to get even more complex. Due
to a long rebuked federal policy, 107 Indian land ownership has often
passed down to multiple heirs in undivided interests. 108 Today, most
Indian allotments have several owners.109 This [fractionated
ownership]{.underline} scheme [makes land use inefficient because it can
require the consent of numerous owners]{.underline}.\" 110
[Getting]{.underline} these [approvals can be **exceedingly
difficult**]{.underline}. Locating each of potentially hundreds of
owners can take an exorbitant amount of time.\"\' Owners also have an
incentive to negotiate for better prices. For example, an allotment with
five or fewer owners cannot proceed without the consent of ninety
percent of the ownership interest,\"112 so a single individual can hold
out for an above market price.\"113 Eminent domain is regularly used to
address holdout problems outside of Indian country;\"114 nevertheless,
solutions to fractionated Indian land ownership have proven more
difficult.\" 115 Hence, the transaction costs associated with
fractionation can render land economically worthless.\"116

Fractionation and bureaucracy are only two causes of land use
uncertainty. A 2009 Supreme Court decision cast a cloud over whether
tribes recognized after 1934 can have reservations,\" and based upon
this decision, the Department of Interior revoked the Mashpee
Wampanoag\'s reservation in 2020.118 Lack of certainty over whether land
qualifies as a reservation deters investment\" 119 because different
rules apply inside Indian country.120 Another issue is [contracts that
encumber Indian lands for seven or more years must be approved
by]{.underline} the Secretary of the [Interior]{.underline}.121
[Secretarial approval is a transaction cost]{.underline}. Moreover, [the
requirement\'s application **adds uncertainty** because \"\[t\]here is
**no clear answer**]{.underline} as [to whether a construction or design
contract is]{.underline} one [\'relative to\' their
lands.\"]{.underline} 122 [Adding **further complication**]{.underline}
to Indian land, [federal law prohibits tribes from selling land
they]{.underline} have [purchased on the private]{.underline} real
estate [market]{.underline}.123 Though the law is seldom enforced,124
[attempting to buy]{.underline} land [from an Indian tribe **subjects
the would-be purchaser to federal sanctions**]{.underline}. 125
[These]{.underline} factors [add **uncertainty to the already tumultuous
rules** governing Indian country land and drive businesses away from
Indian country]{.underline}.

[If a business manages to lease trust land, the business **must** then
**obtain an Indian trader license** to lawfully operate]{.underline} in
Indian country.126 [Acquiring the license requires the business to
establish its qualifications]{.underline} to operate in Indian country
[including the amount of capital to be invested as well as]{.underline}
the applicant\'s [relevant business experience]{.underline}.127 [A
license only applies to a single location]{.underline} within a
reservation 128 [and is nontransferable]{.underline}.129 Thus, [transfer
or expansion add the transaction cost of applying for an entirely
new]{.underline} Indian trader [license]{.underline} to cover the same
business.

Once the license is obtained, there are additional operational
considerations. The [business cannot purvey]{.underline}-[or even
possess]{.underline}-[**any goods** the federal government provides to
Indians]{.underline}.130 The [price]{.underline} of items sold by
licensed traders [must be \"fair and reasonable\" as determined by a
**federal inspection rather than the market**]{.underline}.131
Additionally, [the establishment of licensed Indian traders must be
managed by]{.underline} someone who is [a habitual resident]{.underline}
of the reservation.132 Although enforcement of the Indian trader
licensing requirement is rare,133 [the penalty for failure to obtain the
license is]{.underline} real-**[forfeiture of all merchandise in the
business\' possession]{.underline}**. 134 Thus, [Indian trader laws
serve as a **perpetual sword of Damocles for businesses operating Indian
country**]{.underline}.135 While Indian trader licenses may have served
a valid purpose in the nineteenth century when many Indians did not
speak English, the [laws serve no purpose]{.underline} in the
twenty-first century [other than **adding transaction costs** and
**uncertainty to Indian country business**]{.underline}.136

#### Onerous regulations block development, [even on trust land]{.underline}. 

Adam **Crepelle 21**, Associate Professor of Law at Southern University
Law Center, Co-Founder of the American Indian Chamber of Commerce of
Louisiana, Citizen of the United Houma Nation, J.D. from the Southern
University Law Center, LL.M. in Indigenous Peoples Law and Policy from
the University of Arizona James E. Rogers College of Law, "White Tape
and Indian Wards: Removing the Federal Bureaucracy to Empower Tribal
Economies and Self-Government," University of Michigan Journal of Law
Reform, Vol. 54, No. 3, Spring 2021, accessed 07-23-2025, HeinOnline, DG

[The bulk of the regulatory trouble lies with trust land. Trust land is
**not freely alienable**]{.underline},73 [so those who wish to use trust
land **must lease it**]{.underline}.74 [Since the federal government
holds title]{.underline} to trust land,75 [obtaining a
lease]{.underline} on trust land [is an **adventure in federal
bureaucracy**]{.underline}. The Bureau of Indian Affairs
([BIA]{.underline}) has [designed specific regulations for business
leases. A business lease]{.underline} on trust land **[cannot take place
without the BIA\'s approval]{.underline}**.

[To obtain]{.underline} the BIA\'s [approval]{.underline} for a business
lease on trust land, [a company must provide a **litany of documents**
including archeological and environmental \"reports, surveys, and site
assessments\"]{.underline} that apply to federal and tribal land\' [as
well as \"a restoration and reclamation plan.\"]{.underline}\' Business
[leases must]{.underline} also [contain a clause requiring the company
to immediately cease activity if a previously unknown cultural item is
discovered]{.underline} on the leased property.80 [If a]{.underline}
business [lease includes permanent improvements, the lease must specify
the location and type of improvement in addition to a construction
schedule]{.underline}.\' [An enterprise that fails to complete a
permanent improvement]{.underline} within the specified time [is
required to explain the cause of delay to the BIA]{.underline}.82 Leases
can be amended to account for changes of circumstances; however, [the
BIA must authorize]{.underline} the [amendment]{.underline}.83 The lease
requirements are different for agricultural, 84 residential,85 and wind
and solar projects.86 The federal leasing regulations can also vary from
reservation to reservation.87

Obtaining a business lease is just step one. [Federal regulations
require non-Indians to **obtain an Indian trader license** to engage in
business relations with Indians on a reservation]{.underline}.8 The
license will not be issued until the lease is obtained.89 To obtain a
license, prospective [licensees must prove they are of good moral
character, have business experience, have the capital to invest, and
more]{.underline}.90 The license only applies to a single store, meaning
[an individual must obtain a separate license to open the same exact
enterprise at different coordinates]{.underline} within the same
reservation.91 [In the event a licensee wishes to sell the
business]{.underline} to another person, [the licensee cannot simply
transfer her license]{.underline}.92 Rather, she must get the BIA\'s
approval for the transfer; otherwise, the purchaser of the business must
go through the hassle of getting the license on his own. [Companies
operating without an Indian trader license may be **shut down by the
BIA**]{.underline},94 and [failure to obtain a license places the
enterprise at risk of forfeiture of \"all merchandise offered for sale
to the Indians or found in his possession]{.underline}.\"95

The bureaucracy continues even after a licensed company opens a store in
Indian country. Within Indian country, [businesses cannot engage in
commerce for-or even possess-any item the **U**nited **S**tates has
provided the Indians for their welfare]{.underline}.96 Businesses cannot
even independently set their own prices, as the Code of Federal
Regulations declares:

> It is the duty of the superintendent to see that the prices charged by
> licensed traders are fair and reasonable. To this end the traders
> shall on request submit to the superintendent or inspecting officials
> the original invoice, showing cost, together with a statement of
> transportation charges, retail price of articles sold by them, the
> amount of Indian accounts carried on their books, the total annual
> sales, the value of buildings, livestock owned on reservation, the
> number of employees, and any other business information such officials
> may desire. The quality of all articles kept on sale must be good and
> merchantable.97

[Establishments operated by licensed traders \"must be managed by the
bonded principal\" who is a **habitual resident of the
reservation**]{.underline} where the business is located.98 [The Indian
trader regulations]{.underline} even [limit the methods by which traders
can pay Indians]{.underline}99 [and **grant the President authority to
block goods from entering Indian reservations**]{.underline}.100 Like
the Indian country leasing regulations, [different federal Indian trader
regulations apply to different reservations]{.underline}. 101

[**As with trust land**, whether a person is Indian impacts how Indian
trader laws apply. \"Full blood\" Indians are exempt from]{.underline}
Indian [trader license requirements]{.underline}.10 2 Only individuals
who are not \"pure Indian\" must obtain the license to conduct business
with Indians on a reservation.103 **[Race is an overt factor for
employees of Indian traders]{.underline}** too, as the law states \"
\[t\]hat no white person shall be employed as a clerk by any Indian
trader, except such as trade with said Five Civilized Tribes, unless
first licensed so to do by the Commissioner of Indian Affairs, under and
in conformity to regulations to be established by the Secretary of the
Interior.\"104 The law does not define \"white person,\" leaving
unanswered: Who is a \"white person\"?105

These [federal regulations make starting a business in Indian country
**much more difficult** than opening a business outside of Indian
country]{.underline}. These **[regulations]{.underline}**0 6
**[needlessly increase transaction costs, adding uncertainty and expense
to Indian country commerce]{.underline}**.107 These regulations-that
apply only to Indian country- are crippling reservation economies.\'
Accordingly, these regulations should be challenged in court as
unconstitutional impediments to tribal economic development. The
following Part examines the unique constitutional position of Indians.

#### They can't facilitate development because of [bureaucratic costs]{.underline}.

[Although trusteeship enables tribes to preserve their land
base]{.underline} as trust land, generally cannot be seized from
creditors, is immune from state taxation, and income earned on trust
land is exempt from state taxation (Crepelle, 2020), [its more general
consequence is to **add costs to economic activities** in Indian
country]{.underline} (Miller, 2012, 2018). [The trouble]{.underline}
with trust status [is the **complex federal bureaucracy** that encumbers
it]{.underline}, thereby [**reducing Indians' ability to find the most
efficient use of their land** even today]{.underline} (Russ and
Stratmann, 2016). The [IRA]{.underline} established [rules]{.underline}
that continue to [severely restrict alienability of
property]{.underline} (Shoemaker, 2016). [Limited alienability
complicates the mortgage process]{.underline}; in fact, [**mortgaging
trust land requires** the **approval** of the Secretary of the Interior,
increasing the burden on owners to use their land as
collateral]{.underline}.12 [Land held in trust is]{.underline} also
[subject to fractionation, which is a problem of tenancy in
common]{.underline} (with restrictions on alienability) [rather than
incomplete assignment of property rights]{.underline}. Although common
law favors partition or sale when multiple parties inherit the same
parcel, [Section 5 of the Dawes Act required]{.underline} that [heirs
received undivided interests]{.underline} (Sawers, 2009), [resulting in
ownership interests passing down through the generations producing
**ever-smaller ownership interests**]{.underline}. According to the
Supreme Court in Hodel versus Irving (1987), **[Indian trust lands
averaged over 100 co-owners]{.underline}** for each plot of land in some
parts of Indian country. According to the Court, '[Because the land was
held in trust and]{.underline} often [could not be alienated or
partitioned, the fractionation problem grew and grew over time'. The
result is an **anticommons problem: excessive assignment of private
property rights undermines the value of an asset**]{.underline} (Heller,
1998), [even if the parcel itself is a reasonable size]{.underline}
(Shoemaker, 2014). [Fractionation renders Indian property]{.underline},
in de Soto's (2000) terminology, **[dead capital]{.underline}**, though
the problem is not simply in the institutional constraints on particular
parcels of land: [the defects in any]{.underline} given parcel of [land
frustrate the]{.underline} necessarily [coordinative reassembly of
land]{.underline} (Alston and Smith, 2020), as [there is not an easy way
for contracting parties to assemble plots of fee simple as many
reservations]{.underline} within any specific location [have a
combination of fee simple, tribal, and trust land]{.underline}.

#### Lack of co-ownership clarity makes it [more complex]{.underline} and [uncertain]{.underline} than the status quo. 

Jessica A. **Shoemaker 17**, Assistant Professor of Law at the
University of Nebraska College of Law, J.D. from the University of

The [trust status itself remains **overly rigid and technical** for

[buy-back efforts result in a **new co-ownership** between fee owners

### Circumvention\-\--1NC

#### Interior circumvents\-\--they will [misappropriate land]{.underline} as the trustee and [privatize lands]{.underline} in response to Indigenous demands.

Kristen A. **Carpenter &** Angela R. **Riley 19**, Council Tree
Professor of Law at University of Colorado Law School, Member from North
America of the United Nations Expert Mechanism on the Rights of
Indigenous Peoples, J.D. from Harvard Law School; Professor of Law and
Director of the Native Nations Law and Policy Center at the University
of California, Los Angeles, School of Law, J.D. from Harvard Law School,
"Privatizing the Reservation?", Stanford Law Review, Vol. 71, No. 4,
April 2019, accessed 07-23-2025, HeinOnline, DG

In popular culture, American Indian [reservations]{.underline} often
[appear as islands of **neglect** and **despair**]{.underline}.1 These
depictions draw from grains of truth, some of them quite devastating.
Last winter on the Pine Ridge Reservation in South Dakota, for example,
a twelve-year-old girl reportedly attempted suicide because she was
freezing.2 Many Indian reservations are plagued by high rates of
poverty, 3 along with substandard housing, poor health, crime, and other
social ills.4 The problems of Indian poverty and living conditions on
reservations have inspired various explanations. But one account,
advanced by economists and commentators,5 has started to gain traction
and is now seen as part and parcel of an apparent desire to
\"privatize\" Indian lands.6 The argument is that reservation-based
Indians don\'t have \"property rights,\" which, in turn, shackles them
and inhibits their ability to create wealth.7 To address this issue, a
long line of scholarship, led most prominently by Terry Anderson, has
argued for increased individuation and alienability-and diminished
federal responsibility-with respect to tribal lands and resources. 8
While the place of Indian lands seems longstanding, even permanent, in
the United States,9 these calls for modifications to the Indian land
tenure system deserve serious consideration. The issue is particularly
pressing in light of recent, significant changes in the political
leadership in the United States.

Most recently, [Indian lands and resources have become **targets of the
Trump Administration\'s development agenda**]{.underline}. 10 One of the
first moves by the Administration was an attempt, still pending in the
courts, to vastly shrink Bears Ears National Monument.11 Under the Obama
Administration, Bears Ears was set aside in a unique tribal
co-management program to protect the petroglyphs, monuments, and
landscapes that are sacred to the tribes of the region.12 But the
designated area is also rich in natural resources, 13 and as the New
York Times recently revealed, the Trump Administration\'s decision to
diminish the National Monument and its protections for tribal culture
was motivated by a desire to foster oil extraction. 14 Many other
[tribes control]{.underline} similar [lands rich in natural resources
that players in the extractive industries would]{.underline} almost
certainly [like to access with less regulatory oversight]{.underline}.15
With respect to Indian reservations, President Trump\'s acting Assistant
Secretary for Indian Affairs testified in favor of [a controversial bill
that would]{.underline}, upon request of an Indian tribe,
[require]{.underline} the [Interior]{.underline} Department **[to take
reservation land out of trust and put it in \"restricted fee\"
status]{.underline}**.16 More recently, the new Assistant Secretary
issued a decision regarding the Mashpee Wampanoag Tribe that \"pave\[d\]
the way for a reservation to be taken out of trust for the first time
since the termination era.\"17

Given that President [Trump has expressed a desire to **privatize
everything**]{.underline} from national parks to air traffic control, 18
and given the unpredictability and volatility of the current political
landscape, it is difficult to know how seriously to take this threat or
how extensive the scope of its impact might be. Yet in light of the
resonance between this political conversation on privatization and the
extensive body of scholarship arguing for more private property rights
in Indian Country, there are important issues worth considering here.
Among other things, [privatization could have **disproportionate impacts
on Indian tribes**, which are already under extreme stress]{.underline},
with conditions only made worse by the recent government shutdown.1 9
Moreover, **[the President has long been on the record criticizing
successful Indian economic development initiatives]{.underline}**.20
Thus, [there is healthy skepticism about whether the advocated
free-market approach to tribal property is based on a genuine concern
for Indians\' wealth or well-being]{.underline}. 21

### Delay\-\--1NC

#### Development takes [decades]{.underline}.

Adam **Crepelle 24**, Assistant Professor of Law at the Loyola

[In addition to complexity over]{.underline} whether [tribal or state
law applies, the federal government burdens Indian country with
regulations that **apply nowhere else**]{.underline} in the United
States, and [this **undermines tribal lawmaking power**]{.underline}.185
[Trust land is a prime example. The federal government holds the
underlying title]{.underline} to it while an individual Indian or tribe
is the beneficiary.1 86 [Performing **virtually any activity** on trust
land **requires federal approval**]{.underline}, and [it can **take
years for the feds to approve something** as basic as a
lease]{.underline}.187 [The federal government asserts extensive
authority over private businesses]{.underline} operating [on
reservations]{.underline} too, and the [regulations]{.underline} can
[vary whether a person is an Indian or not]{.underline}.188 While tribes
have the authority to establish their own trust land leasing regulations
under the Helping Expedite and Advance Responsible Tribal Home Ownership
Act (HEARTH Act), the federal government requires the tribal leasing

### Alt Cause\-\--1NC

#### Other barriers to development outweigh the aff.

Adam **Crepelle 24**, Assistant Professor of Law at the Loyola

[Funding influences the development of tribal law. Hiring]{.underline}

## Norway CP\-\--AFF

### No Svalbard War\-\--2AC

#### No Norway-Russia war\-\--robust [cooperation]{.underline} and [mutual interests]{.underline}.

Andreas **Østhagen 18**, Senior Fellow at The Arctic Institute, Senior
Research Fellow at the Fridtjof Nansen Institute, Ph.D. in International
Relations from the University of British Columbia, "How Norway and
Russia avoid Conflict over Svalbard," The Arctic Institute, 06-19-2018,
accessed 02-02-2021,
https://www.thearcticinstitute.org/norway-russia-avoid-conflict-svalbard,
DG

In 1977, Norway established a maritime Fisheries Protection Zone (FPZ)
around the Arctic Archipelago of Svalbard. [[Norway avoided]{.mark}
claiming an outright]{.underline} Exclusive Economic Zone
([[EEZ]{.underline}]{.mark}) [fearing it would be challenged due
to]{.underline} stipulations (or lack thereof) concerning extended
maritime zones in [the Svalbard Treaty]{.underline} from 1920.1) A
dispute with Russia over the status of the Zone arose, although the two
countries quickly agreed to disagree as long as Russian fishermen would
have access to fisheries in the Zone.

In the late 1990s, Norwegian fisheries enforcement in the FPZ became
stricter, in tandem with declining fish stocks and fear of considerable
illegal, unreported, and unregulated (IUU) fishing in the Barents Sea.
This led the Norwegian Coast Guard to attempt to arrest Russian fishing
vessels on several occasions, resulting in reactions from Russian
fishermen, as well as officials in Murmansk and Moscow.2) In 1998, 2001,
2005, and 2011 specifically, incidents had the potential to escalate
beyond a fisheries issue.

Today, the [[risk of events spiralling]{.mark} out of
control]{.underline} in the FPZ [[remains a]{.mark} primary
[concern]{.mark} for both the Norwegian Coast Guard and]{.underline} the
Norwegian [Ministry of Defence.]{.underline} Echoing these fears, in
2017 the Russian Defence Ministry deemed Svalbard a potential area for
future conflict with Norway, and in consequence NATO.3) Given the
potential volatility of events in the FPZ, how do **[[Norway and
Russia]{.underline}]{.mark}** manage to **[[avoid escalation]{.mark} in
the case of a crisis]{.underline}**? Two intertwining relationships are
integral to answering this.

Interest alignment

First, [**coop**eration **can be the product of discord**]{.underline}.
In other words, states cooperate when they disagree. Sometimes
[[institutions]{.mark} are needed to [overcome]{.mark} the [self-help
tendencies]{.mark} of the international system]{.underline}. Along this
line of argumentation, [cooperation between Norway and Russia
[concerning]{.mark} shared [fish stocks, oil spill response,]{.mark}
search and rescue, and ship traffic management are formalised
arrangements]{.underline} set up [to [manage]{.mark}]{.underline}
potential areas of [[discord]{.underline}]{.mark}. Along the same line
of argumentation, the Barents Sea maritime boundary agreement in 2010
was a result of interests between the two states converging
sufficiently.4)

In other words, **[the reason why [Norway and
Russia]{.mark}]{.underline}** manage to **[[keep]{.mark} crises from
escalating]{.underline}** further **[is [mutual interest in]{.mark}
preserving]{.underline}** aspects of their
[**[coop]{.mark}**eration]{.underline}; cooperation that benefits both
parties. This relates to multiple issue areas. Concerning resource
management, Russia is assumed to be satisfied with the current quota
scheme, in contrast to what might be achieved if an actual debate over
the status of the FPZ took place.5) Concerning the UNCLOS-regime,
[[both]{.mark} parties]{.underline} [[benefit from]{.mark} a [stable
legal regime]{.mark}]{.underline} in the FPZ specifically, but also [[in
the Arctic]{.underline}]{.mark} at large.6) And concerning national
security, [[both]{.underline}]{.mark} parties [[have]{.mark} an
[interest in low levels of conflict for fear of]{.mark} coercive efforts
by [other actors]{.mark} within and outside the Barents/Arctic
region]{.underline}.7)

The interests that drive cooperation might, however, be subject to
change (over time). We can see changes taking place within all the
issue-areas addressed: as climate change continues to alter the marine
resource base in the Barents Sea; as external actors challenge the
validity of the UNCLOS-regime in the Arctic; or as the power relations
between various actors fluctuate. The institutions set up to manage
cooperation are also challenged and questions of institutional
resilience become salient: how will cooperative mechanisms adapt as
conditions influencing the premise for cooperation change?8)

For example, as Norway and Russia struggled to reach agreement on a
reduction of annual quotas for the Barents Sea in the early 2000s,
several institutional measures were taken. In 2002, a harvest control
rule was introduced in the Joint Fisheries Commission to set quotas
according to scientific advice while also limiting fluctuations from
year to year. This has been described as the most important change in
the work of the Commission, removing a source of friction between the
two states (which had spill-over effects into the FPZ).9)

Talking to your neighbour

Second, [as emphasised by officials in the Norwegian Coast
Guard]{.underline}, former Coast Guard officers, [and]{.underline}
multiple [scholarly works]{.underline} written on the management of
shared fish stocks in the Barents Sea, **[[personal contact between
individuals constitute]{.mark} a [core]{.mark} element [of conflict
management]{.mark}]{.underline}** in the Barents Sea. [The importance of
[dialogue]{.mark} during a crisis is given]{.underline} much [credit for
**[solv]{.mark}**ing [incidents between the Norwegian Coast Guard and
**Russia**]{.mark}n fishing vessels]{.underline}. This includes
communication between the Coast Guard and Russian trawlers (including
the use of Russian interpreters); between the Norwegian Joint Military
Headquarters and the Chief of the Russian Northern Fleet at Severomorsk;
and between the negotiating groups in the Joint Fisheries Commission.

[Having various means of communicating, and keeping channels
open]{.underline} as a crisis is unfolding, [constitute central elements
in the management of conflict]{.underline} in the FPZ. This can be
linked to the notion of 'track two diplomacy', albeit without an
immediate large-scale international crisis as an impetus. Still,
**[[instilling]{.mark} so called low-level or '[soft'
dialogue]{.mark}]{.underline}** in addition **[[to]{.mark} other more
[formal]{.mark} [channels]{.mark}]{.underline}** of communication,
**[have]{.underline}** -- in this case -- **[[provided]{.mark} an
[effective]{.mark} approach to [conflict
management]{.mark}]{.underline}**.10)

This concerns the larger concept of personal relations, as social
contact between two or more individuals representing different sides in
a dispute. The theory of socialisation emphasises not only the
communication component, but also the fact that a change occurs between
the parties. Norms (expected behaviour) socialise (change) actors
according to a logic of appropriateness.11) [[By interacting]{.mark}
regularly [with the same individuals on the same
issues]{.mark}]{.underline} (fisheries, quotas, inspections) [over time,
[mutual understanding emerges as]{.mark} the
[individuals]{.mark}]{.underline} themselves [better [comprehend the
position of the other]{.mark} party.]{.underline}

Maintaining low tension

[[Personal relations]{.underline}]{.mark} have [formed]{.underline} the
basis for the [institutionalisation of]{.underline} some of the
[mechanisms]{.underline} described above, [that [enable
**coop**]{.mark}eration between Norway and Russia]{.underline} in this
specific instance. Examples include the exchange of coast guard officers
from Norway and Russia, as well as the formalised sharing of information
and annual meetings between the Chiefs of the coast guards. This
institutionalisation and its relevance for the issues at hand in the
Barents Sea at large, and the FPZ specifically, played a central part in
sheltering the coast guard cooperation from sanctions and restrictions
put in place between Norway and Russia in 2014.12) **[[There
is]{.underline}]{.mark}** thus [**[a feedback loop]{.mark} taking place
[between institutionalisation and personal
interaction]{.mark}**s**.**]{.underline}

This article does not attempt to outline a monocausal explanation for
success in maritime dispute management. Instead, the aim is to showcase
how [multiple explanations]{.underline} [emphasising different aspects
of conflict relations contribute to the outcome]{.underline}. In sum,
several factors concerning the dispute in the Svalbard Zone help explain
why it has been managed adequately in the context of a larger quarrel.

Looking ahead, [the writing on the wall is relatively clear: to maintain
low tension in the zone around Svalbard, [Norway and
Russia]{.mark}]{.underline} need both be aware of the factors that
[[keep]{.mark} their [relations civil]{.mark}]{.underline}[.]{.mark}
Upholding dialogue between the two countries' coast guards regardless of
fluctuating international relations is crucial. So is establishing and
re-developing cooperative mechanisms such as the Joint Fisheries
Commission, as the resource situation in Arctic waters might change.

### No Svalbard War\-\--1AR

#### Dialogue prevails. 

Wrenn Yennie **Lindgren &** Nina **Græger 17**, Research Fellow at the
Norwegian Institute of International Affairs, Associate Fellow at the
Swedish Institute of International Affairs, Ph.D. candidate in
International Relations at Stockholm University, M.A. in International

http://dx.doi.org/10.22459/GA.06.2017.07, DG

Whereas the end of the Cold War implied that 'for most other states than
Russia the region has remained either marginal or peripheral', the
[[importance of the High North]{.underline} [was
revitalised]{.underline}]{.mark} [in Norway]{.underline} with the
Norwegian High North strategy from 2005 onwards.73 The [significant
Russian military build-up]{.underline} on the Kola Peninsula from the
mid-2000s also [raised concerns]{.underline} about security among
Norwegian politicians, diplomats and militaries. In particular, the
Russian reopening of old military bases, the increased level of military
exercises and, not least, the increase in overflights of Russian bombers
and strategic bombers off the coast of Norway sparked a new round of
'New Cold War' rhetoric and media headlines.

[Certainly, a more aggressive Russian foreign policy]{.underline}
towards Eastern Europe and Ukraine in 2014 in particular
[has]{.underline} also [put the High North]{.underline}, where Russia
has 'geo-political and military-strategic interests', [back at the top

[a]{.mark} direct [threat to Norway]{.mark}]{.underline}**, at least not

emphasises the safety aspect of Arctic activity.]{.underline}

All of the peacetime, **[[cooperative]{.mark} military bilateral and
multilateral [arrangements]{.mark} in the Arctic [are between]{.mark}
the nation [states]{.mark}]{.underline}** in the region. For instance,
the Ilulissat Declaration of 2008, signed by the five Arctic coastal
states, established a common framework for maritime sovereignty in the
Arctic Ocean. All [[signatories]{.underline}]{.mark} have
[[agreed]{.underline}]{.mark} that [[claims and disputes
